The Mu'tazila Theory of Tawlid and the Ash'ari Refutation via Tadhakkur
Mu'tazila theory of tawlid and Ash'ari refutation via tadhakkur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of tawlid, how they applied it to nazar, and the Ash'ari argument from tadhakkur that refutes it.

Core Idea
If recollection doesn't generate knowledge, why would initial reflection?

This passage introduces the Mu'tazila theory of causal generation (tawlid), which holds that one act necessarily produces another. They apply this to nazar (reflection), claiming it generates knowledge. The Ash'ari response uses an elegant analogy: recollection (tadhakkur) of a previous reflection does not generate knowledge by consensus, so initial reflection should be analogous. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of tawlid
Read the definition and example: hand movement causing key movement. Note that tawlid means one act necessitates another act by the same agent.
The example clarifies the causal relationship.
2
See how Mu'tazila apply tawlid to nazar
They consider nazar an act of the beholder that generates knowledge. Note the clarification that 'act' here means the effect, not the act itself, to preempt objections.
3
Grasp the Ash'ari argument
The Ash'ari argue that tadhakkur (recollection) of nazar does not generate knowledge by agreement, so by analogy, initial nazar also does not generate knowledge, since both are acts of reflection (nazariyya).
This argument effectively refutes tawlid in this specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند المعتزلة بطريق التوليد ومعناه أن يوجب فعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر كحركة اليد الحركة المفتاح، فالنظر على أي تفسير فسر فعل للناظر يوجب فعلاً آخر هو العلم، إذ معنى الفعل ههنا الأثر الحاصل بالفاعل لا نفس التأثير، ليرد الاعتراض بأن العلم ليس بفعل وكذا النظر على أكثر التفاسير، ألا ترى أن الحركة أيضاً ليست كذلك؟ وقد اتفقوا على أن حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح فعلان الفاعل واحد واحتج بعض أصحابنا بعد إبطال التوليد مطلقاً على بطلانه ههنا بأن تذكر النظر لا يولد العلم اتفاقاً، فكذا النظر ابتداء لاشتراكهما في النظرية
And according to the Mu'tazila, by way of generation (tawlid), its meaning is that an act of an agent necessitates another act, like the movement of the hand the movement of the key. Thus nazar, on any interpretation it is interpreted, is an act of the beholder that necessitates another act which is knowledge, since the meaning of 'act' here is the effect produced by the agent, not the act itself, to refute the objection that knowledge is not an act and likewise nazar according to most interpretations. Do you not see that movement also is not such? And they agreed that the movement of the hand and the movement of the key are two acts of one agent. And some of our companions argued, after nullifying tawlid absolutely, against its nullity here, that the recollection (tadhakkur) of nazar does not generate knowledge by agreement, so likewise initial nazar, due to their sharing in being reflective (nazariyya).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila theory of generation (tawlid), which holds that an act of an agent necessarily produces another act, like the movement of a hand producing the movement of a key. According to this, nazar is an act of the beholder that generates knowledge. The author then clarifies that 'act' here means the effect produced by the agent, not the act itself, to avoid the objection that knowledge is not an act. Then it reports that some of our Ash'ari scholars, after completely refuting tawlid, argue against its application here specifically. They argue that recollection (tadhakkur) of a previous nazar does not generate knowledge by agreement, so initial nazar also does not, due to both being acts of reflection (nazari). The argument is that if nazar were generative, then tadhakkur would also be generative, but it is not, so nazar is not. This is a clear analogy.

Key Terms
التوليد
التذكر
النظر
النظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar generates knowledge through tawlid (causal generation).
Correction: According to the Ash'aris, tawlid is invalid, and specifically for nazar, the analogy with tadhakkur shows that nazar does not generate knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise in the Ash'ari argument against tawlid of nazar?
A) That tadhakkur generates knowledge by consensus.
B) That tadhakkur does not generate knowledge by consensus, so nazar should be the same.
C) That nazar is not an act of the servant.
D) That knowledge is created directly by Allah.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument uses the analogy of recollection to show that nazar does not generate knowledge, refuting the Mu'tazila theory of tawlid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own reflection cannot generate true knowledge about God, where do you actually place your trust—your intellect or divine revelation? What does that reveal about the reality of your īmān?
?
You have likely relied on your reasoning to reach conclusions about your faith. After learning that even the act of recollection does not produce knowledge, are you willing to acknowledge that your intellectual efforts are ultimately dependent on God's mercy? If so, what will you change today?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could secure knowledge through their own acts. Have you, in your pursuit of certainty, ever assumed that your study or contemplation alone guarantees truth—without humbly seeking divine guidance? What step will you take to correct that?

Refuting Platonic Forms as Alternatives to Mental Existence
Taftazani rejects the claim that universals subsist by themselves as Platonic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of Platonic forms or other external subsistence for universals does not undermine the necessity of mental existence for non-existents, especially impossibilities.

Core Idea
Impossible things have no quiddity, so they cannot subsist anywhere but in the mind.

Taftazani confronts a philosophical objection: perhaps universals like 'impossible' exist as self-subsisting Platonic forms, suspended forms in the world of images, or impressions in the Active Intellect. He argues that these alternatives are irrelevant because the subject is non-existents that have no quiddity. This lesson presents the objection and his incisive response, showing why mental existence is the only viable option.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it starts with 'If it is said' – that's the objector. Taftazani's response begins with 'We said'. Identify the core claim the objector makes: that universals need not exist in the mind.
The objector offers three alternatives: Platonic forms, suspended forms, and impressions in the Active Intellect.
2
Examine Taftazani's key move
Taftazani narrows the scope to impossibilities (al-mumtani'at). He argues that impossibilities cannot subsist by themselves or in the Active Intellect because they lack a quiddity (hūwiyya). Ask yourself: why is lacking a quiddity fatal to those alternatives?
The impossible has no essence to exist independently; at most, the Active Intellect can conceive it.
3
Connect the argument back to mental existence
Taftazani concludes that the objector's alternatives actually support mental existence: conceptualization (ta'aqqul) requires the form to be in the faculty of the intellect. Trace this final step even though the text cuts off.
The incomplete sentence hints that intellection is by the obtaining of the form in the faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : في رد هذا الجواب أن المعقولات التي لا وجود لها في الخارج لا يلزم أن تكون موجودة في الذهن لجواز أن تكون صوراً قائمة بأنفسها، كالمثل المجردة الأفلاطونية على ما سيأتي. في بحث الماهية، وكالمثل المعلقة التي يقول بها بعض الحكماء، زعما منهم. لكل موجود شبحاً في عالم المثال. ليس بمعقول ولا محسوس على ما سيأتي في آخر المقصد الرابع : أو قائمة ببعض المجردات كما تدعيه الفلاسفة من ارتسام صور الكائنات في العقل الفعال، وينبغي أن يكون هذا مراد الإمام بالأجرام الغائبة عنا، وإلا فقيام المعدومات بالأجسام مما لا يعقل. قلنا : الكلام في المعدومات سيما الممتنعات ولا خفاء في امتناع قيامها بأنفسها بحسب الخارج، ولا بالعقل الفعال بهوياتها، إذ لا هوية للممتنع بل غاية الأمر أن يقوم به تصورها بمعنى تعقله إياها، وهو يستلزم المطلوب من جهة استلزامه كون التعقل بحصول الصورة في القوة
If it is said: In refutation of this answer, the intelligibles that have no existence in the external world need not be existing in the mind, because it is possible that they are forms subsisting by themselves, like the abstract Platonic Forms as will come in the discussion of quiddity, and like the suspended forms which some philosophers affirm, claiming that every existent has a specter in the world of images, neither intelligible nor sensible as will come at the end of the fourth goal; or subsisting in some abstract substances as the philosophers claim regarding the impression of images of generated things in the Active Intellect. And it is fitting that this is what the Imam meant by bodies absent from us; otherwise, the subsistence of non-existents in bodies is not intelligible. We said: The discussion is about non-existents, especially impossibilities, and it is no secret that their subsistence by themselves externally is impossible, nor in the Active Intellect by their quiddities, because the impossible has no quiddity; rather the utmost is that its conceptualization subsists in it, meaning its intellection, and this entails the desired conclusion insofar as it entails that intellection is by the obtaining of the form in the faculty.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector (likely a philosopher) proposes alternative ways for universals to exist: as Platonic forms (self-subsisting abstract forms), as suspended forms in the world of images, or as impressions in the Active Intellect. The author (Taftazani) answers that the discussion concerns non-existents, especially impossibilities, which cannot have quiddities (essences) to subsist by themselves or in the Active Intellect. The most that can be said is that the Active Intellect has a conceptualization (tasawwur) of them, which requires the form to be in the intellect—thus confirming mental existence. The answer is cut off but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
المثل الأفلاطونية
المثل المعلقة
العقل الفعال
الماهية
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Platonic forms provide a real alternative to mental existence for all universals, including impossibilities.
Correction: Taftazani shows that impossibilities lack quiddity, so they cannot subsist as Platonic forms or in the Active Intellect; their only mode of existence is as concepts in the mind.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani reject the idea that universals could subsist in the Active Intellect by their quiddities?
A) Because the Active Intellect does not exist.
B) Because impossible things have no quiddity to subsist there.
C) Because the Active Intellect is material.
D) Because universals are particular.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes alternatives to mental existence for impossibilities by noting they lack quiddity, thus mental existence is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that impossibilities have no quiddity and can only exist as mental concepts. Consider a false belief about Allah or His religion that you once held. Where did that belief truly exist? In your mind alone, as a construct with no external reality. How does this insight compel you to actively replace such false mental concepts with the truth from revelation, rather than letting them passively linger? What specific step will you take today to uproot a baseless idea you may still entertain?
?
The passage argues that even the concept of an impossibility is real only in the mind. Think of the sheer gravity of shirk (associating partners with Allah). It is the ultimate impossibility, yet it becomes a reality in the minds of those who believe it. Does the fact that it has no quiddity outside the mind make its evil lighter or heavier? What does this teach you about your responsibility to guard your own thoughts and to call others to pure tawḥīd, knowing that the most dangerous ideas are those that have no external reality but can corrupt the soul?

Mutual Dependence Without Circularity
Explains mutual dependence of hyle and form from different aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how hyle (prime matter) and form are mutually dependent without circularity, by virtue of different aspects of need.

Core Idea
Mutual dependence without circularity: each needs the other, but from a different respect.

Sanusi introduces a key principle of physical composition: the two components of a body—hyle and form—are interdependent. But how can A need B and B need A without logical circularity? The answer lies in the different aspects of their need, which this passage clarifies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice that the author mentions 'different respects' to avoid circularity.
2
Identify the two respects
Find the two statements: form depends on matter for its specification (individuation), and matter depends on form for its actuality (specification through action).
The passage says form is specified by determinate matter (as receptive), and matter is specified by absolute form (as active).
3
Understand why no circularity
Reflect on why this mutual dependence does not cause circular reasoning: the dependence is from different aspects—one is receptive, the other is active. Thus, there is no vicious cycle.
Circularity would require the same respect of dependence; here the aspects differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وكالهيولي والصورة المفتقر كل منهما إلى الآخر باعتبار . يعني أن الاحتياج فيما بين الجزأين قد يكون من الجانبين، لكن لا باعتبار واحد، وإلا يلزم الدور، وذلك كالهيولي والصورة للجسم، فإن تشخص الصورة يكون بالمادة المعينة، ومن حيث هي قابلة لتشخصها وتشخص المادة بالصورة المطلقة ومن حيث هي فاعلة لتشخصها وسيجيء بيان ذلك .
He said: And like hyle and form, each of which is in need of the other in a certain respect. That is, the need between the two parts may be from both sides, but not in one respect, otherwise circularity would be necessary. That is like hyle and form for the body, for the specification of form occurs by determinate matter, insofar as it is receptive to its specification, and the specification of matter occurs by absolute form, insofar as it is active for its specification. And its explanation will come later.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage discusses the mutual dependence between hyle (prime matter) and form, two components of a physical body. The author states that each needs the other, but the need is not in the same respect; otherwise circularity (dawr) would result. Specifically, form becomes specified (gets its particular identity) through a determinate matter that is receptive to that form, while matter becomes specified (gains actuality) through form that acts upon it. Thus, the dependence is reciprocal but from different perspectives: form depends on matter for individuation, matter depends on form for actuality. This avoids logical circularity because the aspect of need is different.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
مادة
تشخص
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If A depends on B and B depends on A, it must be circular and therefore impossible.
Correction: Circularity only occurs if the dependence is in the same respect. Here, the dependence is from different aspects (receptive vs. active), so it is valid.

Checkpoint
How does the passage avoid circularity in the mutual dependence of hyle and form?
A) By denying that they depend on each other.
B) By stating that the dependence is from different respects.
C) By asserting that one depends on the other unilaterally.
D) By introducing a third element.

Key Takeaway
Hyle and form are mutually dependent but from different aspects, thus avoiding logical circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there relationships where you depend on others and they depend on you? Are those dependencies from the same aspect or different? If the same, might that be leading to a 'circular' stalemate? What can you learn from the hyle-form relationship about healthy dependence?
?
You are a composite of body and soul, each depending on the other. Consider: does your body serve your soul, or your soul serve your body? Are they in the same 'respect' of need, or different? How does recognizing the distinct aspects of dependence help you prioritize spiritual growth over material desires?

Essences Are Made: The First Proof from Possibility
The author states his position and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that essences are made based on the principle that the cause of need for a maker is possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility, not composition, is the real reason essences need a maker.

We begin the fourth investigation into whether essences (māhiyyāt) are made (majʿūla). The author takes the position that all essences are made, contrary to philosophers and Muʿtazila. The first proof establishes that the cause of need is possibility, an attribute inherent to every essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage that opens the investigation and presents the first proof. Then read the translation.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Understand the position
The author says essences are made, opposing philosophers and Muʿtazila. Note that some exempt simple essences.
The disagreement is not just with philosophers but also with the Muʿtazila.
3
Grasp the first proof
The cause of need is possibility. Understand why possibility is an attribute of the essence itself, whether composite or simple.
Possibility is a property of the essence in relation to existence.
4
Note the extra clarification
The author adds that the distinction between the sum of existents and the existence of the sum is unintelligible externally. This addresses an objection.
This clarification refutes a possible objection about the whole's relation to parts.
5
Ask yourself: why does this proof matter?
Consider how this proof shows that every possible essence needs a maker, including simple ones.
The proof undermines the idea that only composite things need a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع الماهيات مجعولة أم لا ..؟ (قال: المبحث الرابع: الماهيات مجعولة (۱) خلافاً لجمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة (۲) مطلقاً (۳)، وللبعض في البسائط لنا وجوه . الأول: أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان وهو صفة للماهية، مركبة كانت أو بسيطة بالنسبة إلى وجودها، والفرق بين مجموع الموجودات ووجود المجموع بحسب الخارج غير معقول) . الثاني : : لا يعقل التأثير إلا في تقرر الماهية. بمعنى صيرورتها تلك الماهية في الخارج، ويلزم منه تقرر الكون()، وذلك لأن المعلول لو تقرر بكماله عند اقتناء الوجود لم يكن للفاعل تأثير . الثالث (٦) : تقرر الماهية ليس بذاتها)، فيكون بالفاعل، ورد الكل بأن مالها الى مجهولية الوجود.
The fourth investigation: Are essences (māhiyyāt) made (majʿūla) or not? He said: The fourth investigation: Essences are made, contrary to the majority of philosophers and the Muʿtazila absolutely, and to some regarding simple essences. We have proofs. First: The cause of need (ʿillat al-iḥtiyāj) is possibility (imkān), which is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, with respect to its existence; and the difference between the sum of existents and the existence of the sum according to the external is unintelligible. Second: Effect (taʾthīr) is only conceivable in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that essence in the external, and it entails the determination of being; because if the effect were determined in its perfection upon acquiring existence, the agent would have no effect. Third: The determination of the essence is not by itself, so it is by the agent. And he refuted all by saying that they lead to the unknownness of existence.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth investigation (al-mabḥath al-rābiʿ) concerning whether essences (māhiyyāt) are made (majʿūla) or not. The author asserts that essences are made, opposing the majority of philosophers and Muʿtazila who hold that essences are not made, and also opposing some who exempt simple essences. Three proofs are provided. First proof: The cause of need (for a maker) is possibility (imkān), which is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, in relation to its existence. The author adds that the distinction between the sum of existents (majmūʿ al-mawjūdāt) and the existence of the sum (wujūd al-majmūʿ) is unintelligible in external reality, likely to counter the philosophers' idea that the whole is not additional to the parts. Second proof: Effect (taʾthīr) is only conceivable in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that specific essence in external existence. This determination entails the determination of being (takwīn). If the effect were fully determined at the moment of acquiring existence, the agent would have no effect, so the determination must be from the agent. Third proof: The determination of the essence is not by itself, because if it were, it would be necessary and eternal. Therefore, it must be by an agent. Then the author mentions a refutation (radd) of all these proofs, stating that they lead to the unknownness of existence (majhūliyyat al-wujūd). This likely refers to the objection that the arguments presuppose a certain notion of existence that is not evident.

Key Terms
ماهيات (māhiyyāt)
مجعولة (majʿūla)
إمكان (imkān)
علة الاحتياج (ʿillat al-iḥtiyāj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only composite essences need a maker, or that essences are eternal.
Correction: The proof shows that possibility is the cause of need, and possibility applies to all essences, even simple ones.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the cause of need for a maker?
A) Composition
B) Possibility
C) Non-existence
D) Complexity

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that essences require a maker because they are possible, and possibility is an inherent attribute of every essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence is possible and dependent on a Maker, do you truly live with the awareness that you are not self-sufficient? Where in your daily life do you act as if you are independent of Allah?
?
The proof shows that no part of you is exempt from needing a Creator—not even your simplest essence. Have you been relying on some aspect of yourself (intellect, skills, status) as though it were self-subsisting? How can you remind yourself constantly of your total dependence on Him?
?
This lesson teaches that possibility is the cause of need for a maker. Does your heart acknowledge that every moment of your existence is contingent on Allah’s will? What needs to change in your prayer, your trust, or your fear to reflect this reality?

The Second Proof: Effect Is Only in Determination of Essence
The author presents the second proof that essences are made.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that essences are made, which argues that an agent's effect is only conceivable in determining the essence in external existence.

Core Idea
An agent's effect is to determine the essence, not just to give existence.

The second proof builds on the idea of effect (taʾthīr). The author argues that the only way to understand the agent's influence is in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that specific essence in the external world. If the effect were already fully determined upon acquiring existence, the agent would have no role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof. Then read the translation.
Notice the logical structure: a claim, a definition, and a conditional argument.
2
Understand the key concept: effect (taʾthīr)
The proof says that effect is only conceivable in the determination of the essence. Understand what 'determination' means here: the essence becoming that particular essence in the external.
Determination (taqarrur) is the act of making the essence actual.
3
Follow the conditional argument
If the effect (the created thing) were fully determined at the moment of acquiring existence, then the agent would have no effect. Therefore, the determination must be from the agent.
This is an argument by contradiction.
4
Connect to the broader proof
This proof shows that the essence itself is the locus of the agent's act, not just existence.
It reinforces that essences are made, not self-existing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع الماهيات مجعولة أم لا ..؟ (قال: المبحث الرابع: الماهيات مجعولة (۱) خلافاً لجمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة (۲) مطلقاً (۳)، وللبعض في البسائط لنا وجوه . الأول: أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان وهو صفة للماهية، مركبة كانت أو بسيطة بالنسبة إلى وجودها، والفرق بين مجموع الموجودات ووجود المجموع بحسب الخارج غير معقول) . الثاني : : لا يعقل التأثير إلا في تقرر الماهية. بمعنى صيرورتها تلك الماهية في الخارج، ويلزم منه تقرر الكون()، وذلك لأن المعلول لو تقرر بكماله عند اقتناء الوجود لم يكن للفاعل تأثير . الثالث (٦) : تقرر الماهية ليس بذاتها)، فيكون بالفاعل، ورد الكل بأن مالها الى مجهولية الوجود.
The fourth investigation: Are essences (māhiyyāt) made (majʿūla) or not? He said: The fourth investigation: Essences are made, contrary to the majority of philosophers and the Muʿtazila absolutely, and to some regarding simple essences. We have proofs. First: The cause of need (ʿillat al-iḥtiyāj) is possibility (imkān), which is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, with respect to its existence; and the difference between the sum of existents and the existence of the sum according to the external is unintelligible. Second: Effect (taʾthīr) is only conceivable in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that essence in the external, and it entails the determination of being; because if the effect were determined in its perfection upon acquiring existence, the agent would have no effect. Third: The determination of the essence is not by itself, so it is by the agent. And he refuted all by saying that they lead to the unknownness of existence.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth investigation (al-mabḥath al-rābiʿ) concerning whether essences (māhiyyāt) are made (majʿūla) or not. The author asserts that essences are made, opposing the majority of philosophers and Muʿtazila who hold that essences are not made, and also opposing some who exempt simple essences. Three proofs are provided. First proof: The cause of need (for a maker) is possibility (imkān), which is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, in relation to its existence. The author adds that the distinction between the sum of existents (majmūʿ al-mawjūdāt) and the existence of the sum (wujūd al-majmūʿ) is unintelligible in external reality, likely to counter the philosophers' idea that the whole is not additional to the parts. Second proof: Effect (taʾthīr) is only conceivable in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that specific essence in external existence. This determination entails the determination of being (takwīn). If the effect were fully determined at the moment of acquiring existence, the agent would have no effect, so the determination must be from the agent. Third proof: The determination of the essence is not by itself, because if it were, it would be necessary and eternal. Therefore, it must be by an agent. Then the author mentions a refutation (radd) of all these proofs, stating that they lead to the unknownness of existence (majhūliyyat al-wujūd). This likely refers to the objection that the arguments presuppose a certain notion of existence that is not evident.

Key Terms
تأثير (taʾthīr)
تقرر الماهية (taqarrur al-māhiyya)
تقرر الكون (taqarrur al-kawn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the agent's effect is only in giving existence, not in determining the essence.
Correction: The proof shows that the agent must determine the essence itself; otherwise, the agent would be superfluous.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the effect were fully determined upon acquiring existence, what would follow?
A) The agent would have no effect
B) The effect would be necessary
C) The essence would be eternal
D) The determination would be unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that the agent's effect is to determine the essence in external existence, so essences are made.

?
In which areas of your life do you subconsciously believe that you determine your own identity? What would change if you sincerely acknowledged that even your character and inclinations are made by the Agent?
?
The proof shows that without the agent, the effect would have no determination at all. In what specific aspect of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient, and what step can you take today to surrend that to the One who truly determines you?

The Third Proof and Its Refutation
The author gives the third proof and then a refutation of all proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that essences are made and the refutation that all proofs lead to the unknownness of existence.

Core Idea
If the determination of an essence were by itself, it would be necessary; so it must be by an agent.

The third proof is a simple but powerful argument: the determination (taqarrur) of an essence cannot be from itself, so it must be from an agent. However, the author also mentions a refutation of all these proofs: they lead to making existence unknown. This refutation raises an important epistemological challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third proof and refutation
Read the Arabic passage that contains both the third proof and the refutation. Then read the translation.
This is the shortest passage, combining a proof and a response.
2
Understand the third proof
The proof states: The determination of the essence is not by itself; if it were, it would be necessary and eternal. Therefore, it must be by an agent.
This is an application of the principle that contingent things need a cause.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation says that all these proofs lead to the unknownness of existence (majhūliyyat al-wujūd). This means the arguments presuppose a notion of existence that is not clear.
This refutation is perhaps from the philosophers or Muʿtazila.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Consider how the three proofs together establish the made-ness of essences, but also note the objection that the entire discussion might be based on an ambiguous concept of existence.

Three Arguments for the Createdness of Essences
Al-Sanūsī enumerates three arguments for the createdness of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three arguments used by Muslim theologians to prove that essences (māhiyāt) are created (maj‘ūla), and how they differ from the positions of philosophers and Mu‘tazila.

Core Idea
Essences themselves need an agent because they are possible.

This passage opens the fourth inquiry of al-Sanūsī’s work, where he sets out to prove that essences themselves are created. He first notes three positions: the theologians hold that all essences are created, the philosophers and Mu‘tazila deny this, and some except simple essences. Then he presents three proofs: the first based on possibility, the second on efficacy requiring determination of the essence, and the third on the non-self-sufficiency of the essence’s determination. A refutation is briefly noted. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the inquiry statement
Read the first two lines where al-Sanūsī states the inquiry and the three positions. Notice the parties involved.
2
Examine the first argument
Read the first argument: the cause of need is possibility, an attribute of the essence. Reflect on why possibility necessitates an agent.
This argument applies to both composite and simple essences.
3
Examine the second argument
Read the second argument: efficacy is only intelligible in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence. Understand why the effect cannot be fully determined upon acquiring existence.
This shows the agent's role in making the essence what it is.
4
Examine the third argument
Read the third argument: the determination of the essence is not by itself, so it is by an agent.
Otherwise it would be necessary, which contradicts its contingent nature.
5
Consider the refutation
Read the final line: all arguments were refuted by leading to the unknownness of existence. Think about why this criticism might be raised.
This sets up the need for further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
الماهيات مجعولة أم لا ..؟
(قال: المبحث الرابع: الماهيات مجعولة (۱) خلافاً لجمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة (۲) مطلقاً (۳)، وللبعض في البسائط لنا وجوه .
الأول: أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان وهو صفة للماهية، مركبة كانت أو بسيطة بالنسبة إلى وجودها، والفرق بين مجموع الموجودات ووجود المجموع بحسب الخارج غير معقول) () .
(٤) ما بين القوسين سقط من (ب) ومن (ج) .
الثاني : لا يعقل التأثير إلا في تقرر الماهية. بمعنى صيرورتها تلك الماهية في الخارج، ويلزم منه تقرر الكون()، وذلك لأن المعلول لو تقرر بكماله عند اقتناء الوجود لم يكن للفاعل تأثير .
(ه) في (أ) نفي الكون بدلاً من (تقرر).
الثالث (٦) : تقرر الماهية ليس بذاتها)، فيكون بالفاعل، ورد الكل بأن مالها الى مجهولية الوجود.
(٦) الثالث من الأوجه التي لنا في إثبات مجعولية الماهية مطلقاً. :
(۷) وإلا كانت واجبة لما تقدم أن المعدوم لو كان شيئاً في نفسبه ثابتاً لكان ثبوته لازماً ذاتياً فيصير واجباً .
The Fourth Inquiry: Are essences created or not? (He said: The Fourth Inquiry: Essences are created (1) contrary to the majority of philosophers and the Mu'tazila (2) absolutely (3), and for some [they are not created] in simple things; we have proofs. First: The cause of need is possibility, and it is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, with respect to its existence, and the difference between the totality of existents and the existence of the totality according to external [reality] is unintelligible. (4) What is between parentheses is omitted from (B) and from (C). Second: Efficacy is not intelligible except in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence, meaning its becoming that essence in the external world, and it entails the determination of being, because if the effect were determined completely upon acquiring existence, the agent would have no efficacy. (5) In (A) 'negation of being' instead of 'determination'. Third (6): The determination of the essence is not by itself, so it is by the agent. And the whole [argument] was refuted by that it leads to the unknownness of existence. (6) The third of the proofs we have for establishing the createdness of essences absolutely. (7) Otherwise it would be necessary, because what has been established is that if the non-existent were something fixed in itself, its fixedness would be an essential necessary attribute, making it necessary.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents the first three arguments of the theologians (mutakallimūn) for the position that essences (al-māhiyāt) are created (maj‘ūla), against the philosophers and Mu‘tazila who deny this. The author begins by stating the inquiry and the three positions: the theologians hold that all essences (both composite and simple) are created; the philosophers and Mu‘tazila hold that they are not created at all; and some hold that only composite essences are created, not simple ones. Then three proofs are given. 

1. The first proof: The cause of need (for an agent) is possibility (al-imkān). Possibility is an attribute of the essence, whether composite or simple, relative to its existence. He adds that the distinction between the totality of existents and the existence of the totality is not intelligible in external reality. The argument is: every possible essence needs an efficient cause because it is possible. 

2. The second proof: Efficacy (ta’thīr) is not intelligible except in the determination (taqarrur) of the essence—i.e., making it become that essence in the external world. This entails the determination of being (kawn). If the effect were already fully determined upon acquiring existence, the agent would have no efficacy; therefore the essence itself must be affected by the agent. 

3. The third proof: The determination of the essence is not by itself (otherwise it would be necessary), so it must be by the agent. 

Then a general refutation is mentioned: all these arguments lead to the unknownness of existence (majhūliyyat al-wujūd), i.e., they make existence unknowable. The footnote adds that otherwise the essence would be necessary, since if the non-existent were something fixed in itself, its fixedness would be essential and it would be necessary.

ماهية
جعل
إمكان
احتياج
تقرر
مجهولية الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'created' here means bringing into existence from nothing; but the arguments focus on the essence itself needing an agent even before considering its existence.
Correction: The theologians argue that the quiddity itself requires an efficient cause, not just its instantiation. Possibility and determination are attributes of the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument for essences being created?
A) Essences are composed of parts that need a composer.
B) The cause of need for an agent is possibility, which is an attribute of the essence.
C) Essences are not perceived by the senses and therefore must be created.
D) The agent's efficacy is limited to giving existence, not determining the essence.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments together show that the necessity of an agent for essences arises from their possibility, the need for their determination, and their inability to determine themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your very essence—your 'whatness'—is not self-sufficient but requires an Agent to determine it, then in what area of your life do you still act as if you are self-made? Will you today trace one attribute you pride yourself on back to its Creator?
?
The philosophers said essences are uncreated, implying they are eternal alongside God. Do you ever treat your own skills or status as if they are eternal possessions rather than gifts given to you at every moment? What would it mean to live each moment receiving your essence anew?
?
The third argument says 'the determination of the essence is not by itself, so it is by the agent.' If you cannot determine yourself to be even what you are, how can you possibly secure your own future or salvation? Where have you placed your ultimate trust—in your own decisions or in the One who gives you your very nature?

The Fourth Argument: The Unknown (al-Majhul)
The author presents the fourth argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for the createdness of essences based on the unknown, and the critical refutation distinguishing specific existence from the essence of existence.

Core Idea
The unknown before creation is specific existence, not the essence of existence.

The fourth argument attempts to prove that all essences are created by listing what is 'unknown' before creation. It claims that the unknown is either the essence, existence, its attribution to the essence, or the joining of parts—all of which are essences, so making any of them entails making an essence. However, the refutation cuts to the heart: the unknown is not the essence of existence but the specific existence itself. This distinction saves the Ash'ari position on the createdness of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the enumeration and the refutation at the end.
The passage includes explanatory footnotes in parentheses; treat them as part of the text.
2
Identify the four candidates
The argument lists four possibilities for the unknown before creation. List them: essence, existence, attribution of existence to essence, and joining of parts. Understand why each is claimed to be an essence.
The claim that 'all are essence' is the key premise.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation says the unknown is 'specific existence' (al-wujud al-khass), not the 'essence of existence' (mahiyyat al-wujud). Grasp why this distinction invalidates the argument.
This distinction prevents the conclusion that making an essence is entailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : المجهول إما الماهية أو الوجود أو اتصافها به أو انضمام الأجزاء(۱)، والكل ماهية (٢) ، ورد بأنه الوجود الخاص لا ماهية الوجود .
(۱) أي انضمام أجزاء الماهية بعضها إلى بعض والانضمام ماهية .
(٢) فيلزم من جعل شيء منها جعل ماهية فيثبت المطلوب .
Fourth: The unknown is either the essence, or existence, or its being described by it, or the joining of parts (1), and all are essence (2). And it was refuted that it is the specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), not the essence of existence (māhiyyat al-wujūd). (1) i.e., the joining of the parts of the essence to each other, and the joining is an essence. (2) So from making any of them, an essence is made, thus the desired is proven.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth argument for the createdness of essences. The argument proceeds by considering what is unknown (al-majhūl) before creation. The unknown can be either: (a) the essence itself, (b) existence, (c) the attribution (ittiṣāf) of the essence by existence, or (d) the joining of parts (in composite essences). The argument claims that all of these are essences, so if any of them is made (maj‘ūl), an essence is made—thus establishing the thesis. However, the response (radd) refutes this by saying that what is unknown is the specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), not the essence of existence (māhiyyat al-wujūd). This distinction undermines the argument because it conflates the act of giving existence (jact) with the essence itself.

Key Terms
مجهول
ماهية
وجود
اتصاف
انضمام
الوجود الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth argument is unanswerable and conclusively proves that all essences are created.
Correction: The refutation shows a critical distinction between specific existence and the essence of existence, which undermines the argument's premise that all candidates are essences.

Checkpoint

A) Essence and existence only.
B) Essence, existence, attribution, and joining of parts.
C) Essence and joining of parts only.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because it conflates specific existence with the essence of existence.

The First Argument from Possibility and the Duality Objection
The theologians' first argument from possibility with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' first argument from possibility for the creation of essences, including the objection that possibility implies duality and the answer that possibility is a relation between essence and existence.

Core Idea
The cause of need is possibility, and possibility is not a relation within the essence.

This lesson presents the core proof that all possible beings—whether simple or composite—require an agent. The theologians argue that the very nature of possibility is what necessitates a creator. But a powerful objection arises: if possibility is a relation, how can a simple essence have it? The author provides a nuanced answer that saves the simplicity of essences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the background dispute
Note the agreement that existence of possible beings comes from an agent, but disagreement about whether the essence itself is created. The theologians hold that all essences are created; philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this.
2
Identify the proof
Both composite and simple essences are possible, and every possible needs an agent because the cause of need is possibility itself.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection claims that possibility is a relation (nisba) that implies duality, contradicting the simplicity of simple essences.
A relation requires two relata, which seems incompatible with a simple entity.
4
Examine the answer
The answer clarifies that possibility is not a relation between parts of the essence, but between the essence and its existence. It means the absence of necessity of existence and non-existence.
Thus, possibility does not compromise simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد الاتفاق على أن وجود الممكن بالفاعل اختلفوا في ماهيته، فذهب المتكلمون إلى أنها بجعل الجاعل مطلقاً أي بسيطة كانت أو مركبة، وذهب الفلاسفة والمعتزلة إلى أنها ليست بجعل الجاعل مطلقاً. بمعنى أن شيئاً جمهور ليس بمجعول، وذهب بعضهم إلى أن المركبات المجعولة دون البسائط. استدل المتكلمون بوجوه : الأول : أن كلاً من المركبة والبسيطة ممكن لأن الكلام فيه. وكل ممكن محتاج إلى الفاعل لما سيأتي : من أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان. ولما اعترض بأن الإمكان نسبة تقتضي الأثنينية (۳) ، فتنافى البساطة (٤) . أشار إلى الجواب بأنه ليس نسبة بين أجزاء الماهية حتى تختص بالمركبة، بل بين الماهية ووجودها، لكونه عبارة عن عدم ضرورة الوجود والعدم. · فمع قطع النظر عن الوجود، لا يعقل تعقل (٥) عروض الإمكان للماهية بسيطة كانت أو مركبة، ومعنى كونه ذاتياً لها، إنها في نفسها بحيث إذا نسبها العقل
(۳) الاثنينية : هو كون الطبيعة ذات وحدتين أو هي كون الشيء الواحد مشتملاً على حدين متقابلين ومتطابقين كتقابل العسكر والعمل في الحالات الثلاث التي يتألف منها قانون التطور الانساني. الحالة الإلهية المطابقة للمجتمع الحربي، والحالة الفلسفية المطابقة للمجتمع الاقطاعي والحالة الوضيعة المطابقة للمجتمع الصناعي . وهي ومن معاني الاثنينية أيضاً كون الشيء مشتملاً على مبدأين مستقلين لا ينحل أحدهما إلى الآخر، كاثنينية الخلقية والخلقية في فلسفة توما الأكويني» أو الهوى والحرية أو الإرادة والعقل، أو الجسم والروح في فلسفة (ديكارات أو الخير والشر، أو النور والظلمة في المانوية (راجع الجمع المنطقي). (٤) في (ب) البسائط. (٥) سقط من (أ) لفظ (تعقل) .
After agreement that the existence of the possible is from the agent, they differed concerning its essence. The theologians held that it is by the making of the maker absolutely, whether simple or composite. The philosophers and Mu'tazila held that it is not by the making of the maker absolutely, meaning that no existence is not made. And some held that composite things are made, but not simple ones. The theologians adduced proofs: First: Each of the composite and the simple is possible, because the discussion concerns it. And every possible is in need of the agent, as will come: that the cause of need is possibility. When it was objected that possibility is a relation that implies duality (3), so it contradicts simplicity (4). He indicated the answer that it is not a relation between the parts of the essence such that it would be specific to the composite, but rather between the essence and its existence, because it is an expression of the absence of necessity of existence and non-existence. So, disregarding existence, the occurrence of possibility for the essence, whether simple or composite, is not rationally conceivable (5); and the meaning of its being essential to it is that it is in itself such that when the intellect relates it... (3) Duality: it is nature having two units or a thing containing two opposite and congruent limits like the opposition of army and work in the three states that make up the law of human development: the divine state corresponding to military society, the philosophical state corresponding to feudal society, and the low state corresponding to industrial society. Also among the meanings of duality is a thing containing two independent principles neither of which reduces to the other, like the duality of creation and morality in the philosophy of Thomas Aquinas, or desire and freedom, or will and reason, or body and soul in the philosophy of Descartes, or good and evil, or light and darkness in Manichaeism (see al-Jam' al-Mantiqi). (4) In (B) 'simple things'. (5) The word 'conceiving' is omitted from (A).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage presents a more detailed version of the first argument from possibility, including an objection and its answer. The author first restates the agreement that the existence of a possible being comes from an agent, but disagreement about whether the essence itself is created. The theologians assert that all essences (simple and composite) are created; the philosophers and Mu'tazila deny this; some hold that only composites are created. Then the first proof is given: both composite and simple essences are possible, and every possible needs an agent because the cause of need is possibility. 

An objection is raised: possibility is a relation (nisba) that implies duality (ithnayniyya)—i.e., it requires two relata—and this would contradict the simplicity of a simple essence, because a simple essence cannot have internal relations. The answer is that the relation of possibility is not between parts of the essence (which would only apply to composites), but rather between the essence and its existence. Possibility is defined as the absence of necessity of existence and non-existence. Thus, when we consider the essence without regard to existence, the attribute of possibility is not a relation within the essence itself; rather, the essence is such that in itself it is non-necessary. The passage ends by stating that possibility is essential (dhāti) to the essence in the sense that the intellect can conceive it as non-necessary.

Key Terms
ممكن
فاعل
إمكان
نسبة
اثنينية
بساطة
علة الاحتياج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about duality shows that possibility cannot apply to simple essences.
Correction: The answer shows that possibility is a relation between essence and existence, not within the essence, so it does not compromise simplicity.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that possibility contradicts simplicity?
A) Because possibility requires a potentiality that cannot be in a simple essence.
B) Because possibility is a relation that implies two things, which cannot exist in a simple essence.
C) Because simple essences are necessary and not possible.
D) Because the theologians only consider composite essences.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is the cause of need for an agent, and it does not imply internal duality because it is a relation between essence and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is purely possible and entirely dependent on the Agent at every moment, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you acknowledge today that you are not self-made, and thank the One who keeps you in existence?
?
The theologians argued that possibility itself is the cause of your need for God. Have you ever considered that your very contingency—the fact that you could have not existed—is the most fundamental proof that you belong to Him? How will you live differently knowing this?
?
When the objection accused the simple essence of being self-contradictory, the answer refocused on the relation between essence and existence. Are you looking for your identity and purpose within yourself (which leads to contradiction) or in your relation to the Creator who gives you existence? Where will you seek your meaning today?

The Second Proof: Agent Must Affect the Quiddity
Sanusi presents the second proof of the mutakallimun regarding the agent's effect on quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof of the mutakallimun that the agent must affect the quiddity itself for existence to be realized.

Core Idea
The effect’s quiddity cannot be fully present without the agent’s action upon it.

This lesson presents the second proof from the mutakallimun for the createdness of quiddities. Before we examine the opponent’s response, we must grasp the reasoning that the agent must intervene in the very essence of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof once. Then read the translation to understand the argument.
The argument uses a conditional: if the effect were already fully present, it would not need an agent.
2
Identify the key claim
Note that the proof asserts the agent must affect the quiddity (الماهية) itself, not just bring existence.
3
Follow the reasoning
The essence of the effect cannot be completely present externally at the moment of receiving existence; something must remain to be produced by the agent, even if it is a structural form.
4
Understand the consequence
If the effect were already fully present, it would exist independently of the agent, negating the agent's causal role and the effect's need for the agent.
This is the key point: the agent's effectiveness is only meaningful if the quiddity itself is not already fully present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الفاعل لا بد أن يؤثر في الماهية ، ويجعلها تلك الماهية الخارج حتى يتحقق الوجود. لأن ذات المعلول عند افنائها (۲) الوجود من الفاعل، لا يجوز أن تكون حاصلة في الخارج بكمالها. بل لا بد أن يبقى (۳) شيء منها يحصله الفاعل، ولو هيئة اجتماعية، وإلا لكان المعلول متحققاً. سواء تحقق الفاعل أو لا ، فلا يكون للفاعل تأثير فيه، ولا له احتياج إلى الفاعل.
الثالث : أنه لا تقرر للماهية في الخارج بذاتها، لما سبق في بحث العدم، فيكون بالفاعل ضرورة ولا معنى لمجعولية الماهية سوى هذا .
والجواب عن الأول : أن معنى احتياج الممكن. أن وجوده ليس من ذاته، بل من الفاعل .
وعن الثاني : أنه لا يدل إلا على أن ماهية المعلول لا تكون حاصلة متحققة بدون الفاعل والحصول والتحقق هو الوجود وهذا لا ينافي كونها متقررة في نفعها من غير احتياج لها وإلى (٤) الفاعل ولا تأثير له فيها .
The second: that the agent must affect the quiddity and make it that quiddity in the external until existence is realized. Because the essence of the effect at the time of its [acquiring] existence from the agent cannot be present in the external in its completeness. Rather, something of it must remain that the agent produces, even if it is a social form, otherwise the effect would be realized whether the agent is realized or not, so the agent would have no effect in it, and it would have no need for the agent. The third: that there is no establishment for the quiddity in the external by itself, as previously in the discussion of non-existence, so it is necessarily by the agent, and there is no meaning for the making of the quiddity other than this. And the answer to the first: that the meaning of the need of the possible is that its existence is not from itself, but from the agent. And to the second: that it only indicates that the quiddity of the effect is not present and realized without the agent, and presence and realization are existence, and this does not contradict its being established in itself without need for the agent and without effect in it.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents two further proofs from the mutakallimun and then the opponent's responses to the first two proofs. The second proof (الثاني) argues that the agent must affect the quiddity itself and make it that quiddity in the external world for existence to be realized. The reasoning is that the effect's essence at the moment of receiving existence from the agent cannot already be fully present externally; something must be produced by the agent, even if it is a structural form (هيئة اجتماعية). If the effect were already fully present, it would exist independent of the agent, negating the agent's causal role and the effect's need. The third proof (الثالث) states that quiddity has no external establishment (تقرر) by itself, based on an earlier discussion about non-existence (العدم), so it must be established by the agent. This is the very meaning of the quiddity being 'made' (مجعولية). Then the opponent's answers (والجواب عن الأول وعن الثاني) are given. To the first proof, the opponent responds that the need of the possible means its existence is from the agent, not its quiddity. To the second proof, the opponent responds that it only proves that the quiddity of the effect is not present and realized without the agent, but presence and realization are existence, which does not contradict the quiddity being established in itself without need for the agent or effect of the agent upon it. Thus, the opponent denies that the proofs establish the quiddity itself as made.

Key Terms
فاعل
ماهية
معلول
تحقق
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would be the consequence if the effect's essence were already fully present externally before the agent's action?
A) The agent would still need to create existence.
B) The effect would be realized without need for the agent.
C) The effect would be impossible.
D) The agent would have to destroy it first.

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that the agent must act upon the quiddity itself, not merely bestow existence, because otherwise the effect would be independent of the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the effect's quiddity is not fully present without the agent, then your very 'self' as you know it is constantly being brought into being by Allah. Do you live with the awareness that at every moment, your existence is being renewed by His act, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The argument says that if the effect were already complete, it would have no need of the agent. When you reflect on your own life, are there areas where you feel no need for Allah—where you think you have it all figured out? How would this proof challenge that complacency?
?
Sanusi argues that the agent must affect the quiddity itself. How does this reshape your understanding of Allah's involvement in your daily affairs? Do you truly believe that every aspect of your being—your personality, your choices, your circumstances—is shaped by His direct action, or do you compartmentalize some parts as 'natural' or 'accidental'?

The Third Proof: No External Establishment Without the Agent
Sanusi presents the third proof that quiddities have no external establishment by themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that quiddities have no external establishment by themselves and therefore must be made by the agent.

Core Idea
Quiddities are not self-established in reality; they depend on the agent for their very being.

The third proof is concise, building on an earlier discussion about non-existence. It argues that since quiddities have no inherent establishment in the external world, they must receive it from the agent—this is precisely what it means for quiddities to be 'made'.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the short Arabic passage for the third proof. Then the translation.
The proof references a previous discussion on non-existence.
2
Identify the premise
The premise is that no quiddity has external establishment (تقرر) by itself.
3
Understand the basis
This is based on an earlier discussion of non-existence (العدم), indicating that quiddities are not inherently existent.
4
Conclude
Therefore, quiddities are necessarily established by the agent, and this is the meaning of them being 'made' (مجعولية).

Source Text

Does this knowledge grip your heart and drive you to constant dependence on Him, or do you still find yourself relying on your own strength? The Opponent's Responses: Need Only for Existence, Not Quiddity
Sanusi records the opponent's answers to the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's responses to the first two proofs, which argue that the need of the possible is only for existence, not for the quiddity itself.

Core Idea
The opponent concedes existence comes from the agent but insists the quiddity is self-established.

After presenting the proofs, Sanusi records how the opponent (the opposing school) answers them. The opponent reinterprets the meaning of need and distinguishes between quiddity and existence. This lesson examines these responses and the central distinction they draw.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's answers
Read the Arabic for the two answers. Then the translation.
There are two answers, one for each of the first two proofs.
2
Understand the first response
The opponent answers the first proof by saying that the need of the possible means its existence is from the agent, not its quiddity.
3
Understand the second response
To the second proof, the opponent says that the proof only shows that the quiddity is not realized (present) without the agent, but realization is existence. So it does not prove that the quiddity itself is made.
4
Grasp the distinction
The opponent consistently distinguishes between quiddity (essence) and existence, affirming that only existence depends on the agent.
This distinction is a key point of contention.

Three Arguments for the Createdness of Essence and Their Refutation
Sanusi presents three arguments for the createdness of essence and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three arguments that claim the essence itself is created only prove that existence is from the agent, not that the essence is made.

Core Idea
The three arguments only show existence is from the agent, not the essence.

This passage presents three arguments that appear to prove the essence (māhiyya) itself is created by an agent. However, each argument is answered by distinguishing between the essence as such and its existence. The lesson traces these arguments and their refutations, clarifying a key point in Islamic metaphysics: only existence is causally dependent on the agent, not the quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the three arguments and responses.
The passage is dense; do not worry about understanding every detail yet.
2
Identify the three arguments
Find the first, second, and third arguments in the passage. Note how each is presented and what each aims to prove.
The first argument is about possibility and the need for an agent; the second about the agent affecting the essence; the third about lack of independent establishment.
3
Examine the refutation of each argument
Read the responses (الْجَوَابُ) for each argument. Notice how each response shifts the focus from the essence to existence.
The responses consistently argue that what is needed from the agent is existence, not the essence itself.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
The passage ends by stating that even if the arguments were sound, they only prove existence from the agent. Reflect on this key distinction.
This is the central insight: the essence as such is not made, only its existence is.
5
Reflect on the essence-existence distinction
Consider why this distinction matters in Islamic theology and philosophy. It preserves the necessary nature of essences while affirming Allah as the giver of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى الوجود يعقل بينهما نسبة هي الإمكان، وهذا المعنى كاف في الاحتياج إلى الفاعل. وقد يجاب : بأنه لو لم تكن البسيطة مجعولة لم تكن المركبة مجعولة، لأنه إذا تقرر في الخارج جميع بسائط المركب حتى الجزء الصوري من غير جاعل، تقرر المركب ضرورة. لا يقال : يجوز أن يكون لكل جزء تقرر ويتوقف تقرر المركب على تقرر المجموع، كما سبق في مجموع التصورات، وتصور المجموع. أنا نقول الفرق بين مجموع التقررات، وتقرر المجموع بحسب الخارج غير معقول، وإنما ذلك بحسب العقل، بأن يتعلق بالأمور المتعددة تارة تصورات متعددة، وتارة تصور واحد من غير ملاحظة التفاصيل. الثاني : أن الفاعل لا بد أن يؤثر في الماهية ، ويجعلها تلك الماهية الخارج حتى يتحقق الوجود. لأن ذات المعلول عند افنائها الوجود من الفاعل، لا يجوز أن تكون حاصلة في الخارج بكمالها. بل لا بد أن يبقى شيء منها يحصله الفاعل، ولو هيئة اجتماعية، وإلا لكان المعلول متحققاً. سواء تحقق الفاعل أو لا ، فلا يكون للفاعل تأثير فيه، ولا له احتياج إلى الفاعل. الثالث : أنه لا تقرر للماهية في الخارج بذاتها، لما سبق في بحث العدم، فيكون بالفاعل ضرورة ولا معنى لمجعولية الماهية سوى هذا. والجواب عن الأول : أن معنى احتياج الممكن أن وجوده ليس من ذاته، بل من الفاعل. وعن الثاني : أنه لا يدل إلا على أن ماهية المعلول لا تكون حاصلة متحققة بدون الفاعل والحصول والتحقق هو الوجود وهذا لا ينافي كونها متقررة في نفعها من غير احتياج لها وإلى الفاعل ولا تأثير له فيها. وعن الثالث : إن أريد بالتقرر التحقق والثبوت فهو الوجود، وإن أريد كون الماهية في نفسها تلك الماهية في الخارج، فلم يسبق ما يدل على أن ذلك بالفاعل، فالوجوه الثلاثة على تقدير تمامها لا تفيد إلا كون الوجود بالفاعل.
To existence, a relation is conceived between them which is possibility (al-imkān), and this meaning is sufficient for the need for an agent (al-fāʿil). And it may be answered: that if the simple (al-basīṭa) were not made (majʿūla), then the composite (al-murakkaba) would not be made, because if all the simples of the composite, even the formal part, are established in the exterior without a maker, the composite is necessarily established. It cannot be said: it is possible that each part has an establishment and the establishment of the composite depends on the establishment of the whole, as previously in the totality of concepts (taṣawwurāt) and the concept of the totality. We say: the distinction between the totality of establishments and the establishment of the whole in terms of the exterior is not intelligible; rather that is according to the intellect, in that sometimes multiple concepts are attached to multiple things, and sometimes one concept without observing the details. Second: that the agent must effect (yuʾaththir) in the essence (al-māhiyya) and make it that essence in the exterior so that existence is realized. Because the essence of the effect (al-maʿlūl) when it acquires existence from the agent, it is not permissible that it be completely present in the exterior. Rather something of it must remain that the agent brings into existence, even a composite form, otherwise the effect would be realized whether the agent is realized or not, so the agent has no effect in it, nor does it need the agent. Third: that there is no establishment (taqarrur) for the essence in the exterior by itself, as previously discussed in the inquiry on non-existence (al-ʿadam), so it is by the agent of necessity, and there is no meaning for the made-ness (majʿūliyya) of the essence other than this. And the answer to the first: that the meaning of the need of the possible is that its existence is not from itself, but from the agent. And to the second: that it only indicates that the essence of the effect is not present and realized without the agent, and presence and realization are existence, and this does not contradict it being established in its own nature without need for the agent and without any effect from it. And to the third: if by establishment is meant realization and subsistence (al-thubūt), it is existence; and if is meant the essence being in itself that essence in the exterior, then nothing has preceded to indicate that this is from the agent. So the three arguments, assuming their completeness, only establish that existence is from the agent.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments for the claim that the essence (māhiyya) is made (majʿūla) by an agent, followed by a refutation of each. The first argument is implied in the opening sentence: the relation of possibility (imkān) between essence and existence necessitates a need for an agent. An objector might say that simple things (al-basīṭa) do not have parts and thus may not need a maker. The response counters that if simples were not made, then composites would not be made either, because if all simples of a composite are already established in the exterior without a maker, the composite is necessarily established on its own. This is defended by distinguishing between the totality of establishments and the establishment of the whole, which is a conceptual distinction, not a real one. The second argument states that the agent must affect the essence to bring it into exterior existence; otherwise the effect would be realized without the agent. The third argument relies on the earlier discussion of non-existence (al-ʿadam): essences have no establishment in the exterior by themselves, so their establishment must come from the agent. The responses refute each: the first redefines 'need' as pertaining to existence, not the essence; the second clarifies that 'realization' is existence, so the agent only brings existence, not the essence itself; the third distinguishes between establishment as existence (which is from the agent) and establishment as the essence in itself (for which no proof is given). Thus the three arguments, even if sound, only prove that existence is from the agent, not that the essence itself is made.

مركبة (murakkaba)
مجعول (majʿūl)
وجود (wujūd)
ثبوت (thubūt)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three arguments conclusively prove that the essence itself is created by the agent.
Correction: The author demonstrates that these arguments only prove that existence is from the agent; the essence as such is not made.

Checkpoint

A) The essence itself is made by the agent.
B) Only existence is from the agent, not the essence.
C) The agent has no effect on the possible.
D) Neither essence nor existence is from the agent.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments, even if sound, only demonstrate that existence is from the agent, not that the essence itself is made.

Or do you sometimes act as if your identity is self-sufficient? How can this lesson transform your understanding of reliance on Allah (tawakkul)?
?
The author argues that the agent only gives existence, not the essence. How does that challenge your assumption about what it means for Allah to be your Creator? Does it make you more aware that every breath and attribute you have is a gift from Him, or does it create a sense of independence?

According to this lesson, your essence (the potential for that quality) is not made by Allah, but its actual existence in you is. Does that humble you? Fourth Argument for the Createdness of Essence and Its Refutation
Argument and response on the made being essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that the made must be the essence and its refutation, which distinguishes between universal essences and particular instances.

Core Idea
The made is a particular individual, not the universal essence.

The fourth argument attempts to prove that the essence itself is created by enumerating all possible effects of a cause and claiming each is an essence. The refutation reveals a critical distinction: the dispute concerns universal essences, not their particular instances. Understanding this distinction is key to refuting the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, four possibilities, a conclusion, and the response.
The argument is compact; read carefully.
2
Identify the four possible effects
The argument lists four things the cause could make: essence, existence, qualification, or joining. Identify each in the text.
These are the four possibilities.
3
Understand the conclusion and the refutation's key move
The argument concludes that the made is the essence because all four are essences. The refutation counters by saying the dispute is about universal essences, not individuals. Grasp this distinction.
The refutation shifts the focus from universals to particulars.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for the broader debate on the createdness of the essence.
This argument is one of several; the refutation preserves the possibility that essences are uncreated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: انه لا نزاع في أن للعلة جعلاً وتأثيراً في الممكن فالمجعول إما الماهية أو الوجود، أو اتصاف الماهية بالوجود، أو انضمام الأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض في المركب خاصة، وكل من الأمور الأربعة ماهية من الماهيات، فيكون المجعول هو الماهية. والجواب : أن النزاع في الماهيات التي هي حقائق الأشياء، لا فيما صدقت هي عليه من الأفراد فيجوز أن يكون المجعول ذلك الشخص الذي هو من أفراد ماهية الإنسان مثلاً، أو الوجود الخاص الذي هو من أفراد ماهية الوجود كذا الاتصاف والانضمام.
Fourth: that there is no dispute that the cause (al-ʿilla) has a making (jaʿl) and effect in the possible, so the made (al-majʿūl) is either the essence or existence, or the qualification (ittiṣāf) of the essence by existence, or the joining (inḍimām) of parts to each other especially in the composite. And each of the four things is an essence among essences, so the made is the essence. And the answer: that the dispute is about the essences that are the realities of things, not about the individuals on which they are predicated. So it is permissible that the made be that individual which is an individual of the essence of man, for example, or the particular existence which is an individual of the essence of existence, and likewise qualification and joining.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument for the createdness of the essence. The argument begins with an uncontroversial premise: everyone agrees that the cause has a making and effect in the possible. The effect of the cause must be one of four things: the essence itself, existence, the qualification of essence by existence, or the joining of parts in composites. Since each of these four is itself an essence (they are all 'whatnesses'), it follows that what is made is the essence. The response refutes this by pointing out a crucial distinction: the dispute is about essences as the 'realities of things' (ḥaqāʾiq al-ashyāʾ), not about the individuals (afrād) that instantiate these essences. For example, 'man' considered as a universal essence is not what is made; rather, the made could be a particular person (fard) who is an individual of the essence 'man', or a particular existence that is an individual of the essence 'existence'. Similarly, qualification and joining are not essences in the universal sense but rather particular instances. Therefore, the argument fails to prove that the universal essence itself is made.

علة
مفعول
اتصاف
انضمام
فرد
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The made must be the universal essence because all possible effects are essences.
Correction: The effect can be a particular individual of an essence, not the universal essence itself. The dispute is about universal realities, not their instances.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the fourth argument, what are the disputed essences?
A) The essences that are the realities of things.
B) The individuals that are predicated by those essences.
C) Both the realities and the individuals.
D) Neither the realities nor the individuals.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because it confuses universal essences with their particular instances; the made can be an individual, not the universal essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The fourth argument confuses universal concepts with particular realities. Is there a belief or practice in your faith that you have accepted as a general principle without examining how it applies specifically to your unique situation?
