Two Meanings of 'Being-Made' and an Objection Answered
Author distinguishes two meanings of maj'ūliyyah and refutes an argument against essence being made.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'being-made' (maj'ūliyyah) and how a key objection against the createdness of essences is resolved by distinguishing existence from essence.

Core Idea
Being-made can mean need for an agent or need for another — a crucial distinction that resolves a major objection.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental disagreement in kalam: are essences (māhiyyāt) 'made' or not? The confusion arises because the term 'maj'ūliyyah' (being-made) has two distinct meanings. By clarifying these, the author not only reconciles differing positions but also refutes a powerful objection against the createdness of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first a clarification of terms, then an objection, then a detailed answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two meanings of maj'ūliyyah
Look for the phrases 'الاحتياج إلى الفاعل' (need for an agent) and 'الاحتياج إلى الغير' (need for another). Understand how each applies to existence and essence respectively.
The first meaning is a property of existence; the second is a property of composite essences.
3
Analyze the objection and its answer
The objection claims that if being human were caused by an agent, then removing the agent would make a human not human. The author gives two interpretations (salb and 'udūl) and refutes each. Trace the logic carefully.
The key is that the agent affects existence, not the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن ليس لها تقرر في الخارج بدون الفاعل لها، فما وجه هذا الاختلاف. أجيب بأنه قد يراد بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى الفاعل وهي من لوازم الوجود كتناهي الجسم دون الماهية كزوجية الأربعة، وقد يراد الاحتياج إلى الغير، فيكون من لوازم الماهية في المركب خاصة، فمن قال: بالمجعولية مطلقاً أراد عروضها للماهية في الجملة، ومن نفاها أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ليس من عوارض الماهية، ومن فصل أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم الماهية المركب دون البسيط، وان اشتركا في احتياج الوجود إلى الفاعل. احتج القائلون بعدم مجعولية الماهية بأن كون الانسان إنساناً لو كان بالفاعل لارتفع بارتفاعه، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان إنساناً على تقدير عدم الفاعل وهو محال. والجواب: أنه إن أريد أنه يلزم أن يكون الإنسان ليس بإنسان بطريق السلب ولا نسلم استحالته، فإن عند ارتفاع الفاعل يرتفع الوجود، وتبقى الماهية معدومة، فيكذب الايجاب فيصدق السلب، وإن أريد بطريق العدول، بأن يتقرر الإنسان في نفسه بحسب الخارج، ويكون لا إنساناً فلا نسلم لزومه، فإن عند ارتفاع الفاعل لا يبقى الإنسان حتى يصلح موضوعاً للإيجاب.
And that it [the essence] has no establishment in the exterior without the agent that brings it about. So what is the face of this difference? It was answered that by 'being-made' (maj'ūliyyah) may be meant the need for an agent (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-fā'il), and it is among the necessary concomitants of existence (lawāzim al-wujūd), like the finitude of a body, not of the essence (al-māhiyyah), like the evenness of four. And it may be meant the need for another (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-ghayr), and then it is among the necessary concomitants of the essence specifically in the composite. So whoever affirmed being-made absolutely intended that it occurs to the essence in some respect, and whoever negated it intended that the need for an agent is not an accident of the essence, and whoever made a distinction intended that the need for another is among the necessary concomitants of the composite essence, not of the simple, while they share in the need of existence for the agent. Those who deny the being-made of the essence argued that if being human were [caused] by an agent, it would cease by the cessation of the agent, so it would follow that a human is not human on the supposition of the absence of the agent, which is absurd. And the answer: if it is intended that it follows that a human is not human by way of negation (salb), we do not concede its impossibility, for when the agent ceases, existence ceases, and the essence remains non-existent, so the affirmative is falsified and the negative is verified. And if it is intended by way of alteration ('udūl), that the human is established in himself with respect to the exterior and is not human, we do not concede its implication, for when the agent ceases, the human does not remain to be a subject for affirmation.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the concept of 'being-made' (maj'ūliyyah) of essences (māhiyyāt). The author first explains the different meanings of maj'ūliyyah to clarify the source of disagreement. He states that the term may refer either to the need for an agent (a property of existence) or to the need for another (a property of the essence, specifically in composites). Thus, those who affirm maj'ūliyyah absolutely include both meanings in some sense; those who deny it mean that the need for an agent is not a property of the essence; and those who distinguish restrict the need for another to composite essences only. Then an objection is presented: if being human were caused by an agent, then annihilating the agent would annihilate humanity, so in the absence of the agent a human would not be human — which seems absurd. The author gives two possible interpretations of the objection and counters each. If the objector means 'not human' by way of simple negation (salb), there is no problem: when the agent is removed, existence is removed, so the essence is non-existent and the negative statement is true. If the objector means 'not human' by way of alteration ('udūl), implying an actual quality contrary to humanity, the author denies the implication: when the agent ceases, no subject remains to be characterized. Thus the objection fails in both interpretations.

الماهية (māhiyyah)
الوجود (wujūd)
الاحتياج إلى الفاعل (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-fā'il)
الاحتياج إلى الغير (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-ghayr)
السلب (salb)
العدول ('udūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that essences cannot be made, because if they were, removing the maker would eliminate the essence itself.
Correction: The objection conflates essence with existence. The author shows that the agent affects existence, not the essence; when the agent is absent, the essence simply does not exist, so the negative statement is true without absurdity.

Checkpoint

A) Need for an agent and need for a part
B) Need for an agent and need for another (al-ghayr)
C) Need for an agent and need for an effect
D) Need for existence and need for essence

Key Takeaway
The term 'being-made' is ambiguous; when clarified, objections against the createdness of essences dissolve.

Al-Iji's Resolution on Maj'uliyyah of Essence
Al-Iji resolves the dispute over maj'uliyyah by distinguishing types of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's key distinction between essence and identity in resolving the debate about whether essences are 'made'.

Core Idea
The need for an agent belongs to identity, not essence — that resolves the dispute.

The debate over whether essences are 'made' (maj'uliyyah) often leads to confusion because the term 'being-made' is ambiguous. Al-Iji clarifies that the need for an agent is a property of the identity (huwiyyah) of a thing, not its essence (mahiyyah). By distinguishing between two types of need — need for an agent and need for a part — he reconciles the opposing positions. Let's read his resolution carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a dismissal of a view, then al-Iji's positive resolution.
The main claim is that need for agent is a concomitant of identity, not essence.
2
Understand the distinction between accidents of essence and identity
The passage distinguishes two kinds of accidents: those inherent to essence (like evenness for four) and those inherent to identity (like finitude for a body). Grasp this distinction.
This is crucial for the resolution.
3
Apply the distinction to need for agent vs. need for another
See how the need for an agent is a concomitant of identity, while the need for a part (another) is a concomitant of composite essence.
This explains why simple and composite essences differ regarding need for a part.
4
See how the various positions are reconciled
The passage shows that each of the three positions (affirmation, denial, distinction) can be interpreted correctly depending on which kind of need is intended.
In the end, no real disagreement remains.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider why this resolution is important for understanding causality and the relationship between essence and existence.

قال: فإن قيل: يريد التنبيه على ما يصلح محلاً للخلاف في هذه المسألة، فإنه معلوم أن ليس للفاعل تأثير، وجعل بالنسبة إلى ماهية الممكن، وآخر بالنسبة إلى وجوده، حتى تكون الماهية مجعولة كالوجود، وأن ليس للماهية تقرر في الخارج بدون الفاعل حتى يكون المجعول: هو الوجود فقط، بل أثر الفاعل مجعولية الماهية، بمعنى صيرورتها موجودة، وما ذكره الإمام من أن المراد أن الماهية من حيث هي هي ليست بمجعولة، كما أنها ليست بموجودة، ولا معدومة ولا واحدة ولا كثيرة إلى غير ذلك من العوارض، بمعنى أن شيئاً منها ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، ليس مما يتصور فيه نزاع، أو يتعلق بتخصيصه بالذكر فائدة. والأقرب ما ذكره صاحب المواقف، وهو أن المجعولية قد يراد بها الاحتياج إلى الغير على ما يعم الجزء، وكلاهما بالنسبة إلى الممكن من العوارض، والعوارض منها ما يكون من لوازم الماهية، كزوجية الأربعة حتى لو تصورنا أربعة ليست بزوج، لم تكن أربعة، ومنها ما يكون من لوازم الهوية، كتناهي الجسم وحدوثه، حتى لو تصورنا جسماً ليس بمتناه أو حادث كان جسماً. ولا خفاء في أن احتياج الممكن إلى الفاعل في المركب والبسيط جميعاً من لوازم الهوية دون الماهية، وأن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم الماهية المركب دون البسيط، إذ لا يعقل مركب لا يحتاج إلى الجزء. فمن قال بمجعولية الماهية مطلقاً، أي بسيطة كانت أو مركبة أراد أن المجعولية تعرض للماهية في الجملة. أعني الماهية بشرط شيء، وهي الماهية المخلوطة، ومرجعها إلى الهوية، وإن لم تعرض للماهية من حيث هي، ويحتمل أن يراد أنه يعرض للماهية من حيث المجعولية في الجملة، أي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغير، وإن لم تكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الفاعل. ومن قال بعدم مجعولية الماهية أصلاً، أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ليس من عوارض الماهية، بل من عوارض الهوية. ومن فرق بين المركبة والبسيطة أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم ماهية المركب دون البسيط، وإن اشتركا في الاحتياج إلى الفاعل بالنظر إلى الهوية. هذا ولكن لم يتحقق نزاع في المعنى.
He said: For it is said: He intends to indicate what is suitable as a locus for disagreement in this issue. For it is known that the agent has no effect and making (ja'l) is with respect to the essence of the possible and another with respect to its existence, such that the essence is made like the existence, and that the essence has no establishment in the exterior without the agent such that the made is only existence. Rather, the effect of the agent is the being-made of the essence, meaning its becoming existent. And what the Imam mentioned, that what is meant is that the essence in itself (min ḥaythu hiya) is not made, just as it is not existent, nor non-existent, nor one, nor many, etc. of the accidents, meaning that nothing of them is itself nor enters into it, is not something in which disagreement is conceivable, nor is there a benefit in specifying it with mention. And the closest [view] is what the author of al-Mawāqif mentioned, which is that 'being-made' (maj'ūliyyah) may be intended as the need for another in a way that includes the part, and both [meanings] with respect to the possible are among the accidents. And among the accidents are those that are necessary concomitants of the essence, like the evenness of four, such that if we conceive a four that is not even, it would not be four; and among them are those that are necessary concomitants of the identity (al-huwiyyah), like the finitude of a body and its temporality, such that if we conceive a body that is not finite or not temporal, it would be a body. And there is no obscurity that the need of the possible for the agent, in both the composite and the simple, is among the necessary concomitants of the identity, not of the essence, and that the need for another is among the necessary concomitants of the composite essence, not of the simple, since a composite that does not need the part is inconceivable. So whoever affirmed the being-made of the essence absolutely, whether simple or composite, intended that being-made occurs to the essence in some respect—I mean the essence conditionally (bi-sharṭ shay'), which is the mixed essence, and its reference is to the identity, even if it does not occur to the essence in itself. And it is possible that it is intended that it occurs to the essence with respect to being-made in some respect, i.e., meaning the need for another, even if it is not in the sense of need for an agent. And whoever denied the being-made of the essence entirely intended that the need for an agent is not an accident of the essence, but rather an accident of the identity. And whoever made a distinction between the composite and the simple intended that the need for another is among the necessary concomitants of the composite essence, not of the simple, although they share in the need for the agent with respect to the identity. This, however, no actual disagreement in meaning has been verified.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is a resolution of the disagreement about the being-made (maj'ūliyyah) of essences, based on the view of the author of al-Mawāqif (al-Ījī). The text first notes that a previous objection attempted to show that the essence cannot be made, but the discussion here clarifies the proper locus of disagreement. The author dismisses the idea that the essence in itself is made, because in itself it is not subject to accidents; that is not genuinely contested. The closest view is that 'being-made' can mean 'need for another' in a general sense that includes the part. The author then distinguishes between two types of accidents: those that are necessary concomitants of the essence (such as 'even' for four) and those that are necessary concomitants of the identity (huwiyyah) (such as finitude and temporality for a body). He states that the need for an agent belongs to identity, not to essence, for both composite and simple things. The need for another (a part) belongs only to composite essences, because a composite necessarily requires its parts. Thus, the various positions are reconciled: those who affirm maj'ūliyyah absolutely mean by 'need for another' in a general sense (which applies to composites via need for parts, and to everything via need for agent but that is not an accident of essence); those who deny it mean that need for agent is not an accident of essence; and those who distinguish mean that need for another (part) is an accident of composite essence but not simple. In the end, the author notes that no real disagreement in meaning remains once the terms are clarified.

المجعولية (maj'ūliyyah)
الماهية (māhiyyah)
الهوية (huwiyyah)
المركب (murakkab)
البسيط (basīṭ)
لوازم (lawāzim)
الاحتياج إلى الفاعل (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-fā'il)
الاحتياج إلى الغير (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'essence being made' means it is created independently of existence.
Correction: The passage shows that 'being-made' means having a need for another (the part) or being mixed with existence, not independent creation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ījī's view as presented in the passage, to which does the need for an agent (al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-fā'il) belong?
A) The essence (māhiyyah) of all possible things
B) The identity (huwiyyah) of possible things
C) The composite essence only
D) The simple essence only

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji resolves the debate by showing that 'being-made' can refer to two different needs — the need for an agent (identity) and the need for a part (composite essence) — and thus the various positions are not in real conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your identity (huwiyyah) as a possible being necessarily includes a need for the Agent (God). But do you live as if your existence is contingent upon Him every moment? Pause and recall a time you felt self-sufficient. How does this lesson expose that feeling as a denial of your very identity? Today, consciously affirm your dependence on God before each action.
?
The passage distinguishes between needing God (agent) and needing others (parts). You are composite—you need people, food, air. But how often do you mistake your dependence on creation for the more fundamental dependence on the Creator? Examine one relationship in your life. Does it overshadow your reliance on God? Resolve to let that relationship point you to Him rather than away.
?
Al-Iji resolved a scholarly dispute by clarifying terms. But the reality of your need for God is not a mere academic point. If you truly believed that your very identity hangs on God each instant, what would change about your prayer, your fears, your hopes? Write one concrete change you will make today to live out this truth.

The Concept of Determination and the Ash'ari View
Definition and Ash'ari view on determination with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of determination (ta'ayyun) and why the Ash'ari school attributes it to the will of Allah.

Core Idea
Determination depends on preventing conceptual sharing and is caused by Allah's will.

In this passage, al-Sanusi introduces the problem of determination: how does a universal concept become a particular individual? He argues that mere conceptual conjunction does not produce determination; rather, according to the Ash'ari view, it is the will of the Omnipotent and Free Chooser that causes determination. The passage also presents an alternative view linking determination to external existence and an objection based on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Focus on the definition of ta'ayyun and its condition.
The opening sentence sets the stage.
2
Identify the three views mentioned
Find the Ash'ari view (will of Allah), the view of 'some' (external existence), and the objection.
3
Understand the objection
Why does the author object to the view that determination comes from existence? What is the role of 'co-variation'?
This objection challenges causal inference.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this lesson connects to the broader theme of divine attributes and causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث التعين يتوقف(۱) على امتناع الشركة (٢) ذهناً، فلا يحصل بانضمام الكلي إلى الكلي ولو بحيث يمنع الشركة عيناً(۳)، بل يستند عندنا إلى إرادة القادر المختار، أو عند البعض إلى الوجود الخارجي، لتحققه قطعاً(٤) ، وتتعدد الأشخاص بتعدد الوجودات .
عنده
ورد بأن الدوران لا يفيد العلية ولو سلم، فالكلام في خصوص التعينات. وعند الفلاسفقة إلى نفس الماهية فينحصر في فرد، أو إلى المادة المشخصة بالأعراض التي تلحقها بحسب الاستعدادات المتعاقبة، فيتكثر بتكثر المواد () القابلة للتكثر بذواتها ، واعترض بأن تعين الأعراض، إنما هو بتعين المادة فتعينها بها دور .
وأجيب بأن تعينها بالأعراض لا بتعيناتها .
قلنا فليكن تعين الماهية بما يخصها من الصفات وتكثر له أفراد :
بتكثرها) .
(1) يتوقف تحققه للشيء.
(2) على امتناع الشركة في ذلك الشيء مما يقبل الشركة بصحة صدقه على أفراد كثيرة لا تعين فيه .
(3) أي في عين مصدوق المضموم والمضموم إليه بأن لا يصلح وجود فردين في الخارج للمنضمين بأن يتعين لهما فرد واحد .
(4) إذ لا يتصور الوجود الخارجي إلا للفرد والفرد له تعين قطعاً.
The third investigation: Determination (al-ta'ayyun) depends on the prevention of conceptual sharing, so it is not obtained by joining the universal to the universal even if it prevents sharing in re, but according to us it is based on the will of the Omnipotent and Free Chooser, or according to some on external existence, because of its certainty, and individuals become multiple by the multiplicity of existences. According to him, it is objected that co-variation does not entail causality, and even if it is accepted, the discussion is about particular determinations. According to the philosophers, it is based on the essence itself, so it is confined to one individual, or on matter individualized by accidents that attach to it according to successive dispositions, so it multiplies by the multiplicity of matters that are capable of multiplication by themselves. It was objected that the determination of accidents is only by the determination of matter, so their determination by it is circular. And it was answered: their determination is by accidents not by their determinations. We say: then let the determination of the essence be by what is particular to it of attributes, and individuals are multiplied by their multiplicity.
(1) Its realization for the thing depends.
(2) On the prevention of sharing in that thing, which admits sharing by the validity of its predication of many individuals without determination in it.
(3) Meaning in the individual instantiation of the conjunct and the conjoined, such that it is not suitable for the existence of two individuals in the external world for the conjoined things, so that one individual is determined for them.
(4) Because external existence is not conceivable except for the individual, and the individual has determination necessarily.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the problem of determination (ta'ayyun), i.e., how a universal concept becomes individuated into particular instances. The author begins by stating that determination depends on preventing conceptual sharing (shirkah dhihniyyah) — meaning that the universal must not be predicable of multiple individuals in the mind. However, simply conjoining universals does not produce determination, even if the conjunction prevents sharing in actual individual instances (ayn). According to the Ash'ari school ("when us"), determination is ultimately due to the will of the Omnipotent and Free Chooser (Allah). Others attribute it to external existence itself, since existence is always concrete and thus determined. Individuals multiply by the multiplicity of existences. An objection is raised against the view that determination is due to existence: co-variation (dawaran) does not prove causality. Even if it did, the discussion is about specific determinations, not the general link. The philosophers' view is then presented: determination is either from the essence itself (so the essence produces only one individual) or from matter individualized by accidents, which vary according to successive dispositions, so individuals multiply by the multiplicity of matters that are by themselves capable of multiplication. An objection is raised: the determination of accidents themselves depends on the determination of matter, so the explanation becomes circular (the determination of matter is by accidents, and accidents by matter). The reply is that the determination of accidents is by the accidents themselves, not by their determinations (i.e., not circular). The author then retorts: if that is the case, then let the determination of the essence be by its particular attributes, and individuals multiply through their multiplicity. The footnotes clarify that determination depends on preventing sharing, and that external existence necessarily implies individual determination.

Key Terms
التعين
الشركة الذهنية
القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think determination is simply a mental process or automatic result of existence.
Correction: The Ash'ari view insists determination requires a real cause, namely Allah's will, not just conceptual or existential factors.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for determination (ta'ayyun) according to the opening of the passage?
A) Joining universals together
B) Prevention of conceptual sharing
C) External existence
D) The will of Allah

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school grounds the determination of individuals in the will of Allah, not in mere conceptual or existential factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every unique individual that exists does so because Allah specifically willed it? Or do you sometimes think that things just 'happen' to be distinct? How does this affect your reliance on Allah?
?
If determination is from Allah's will alone, then your identity, your circumstances, your very existence is a direct act of divine choice. How does that change how you view your purpose and your responsibility?
?
Consider a blessing in your life that you think you 'earned' or 'deserved.' After this lesson, can you honestly attribute its determination solely to Allah's will, or do you still feel a sense of entitlement? What would it take to internalize that it is entirely from Him?

Philosophical Theories of Determination and Their Critique
Philosophical theories and their critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical views on determination and the circularity objection.

Core Idea
Philosophers explain determination either by essence or by matter and accidents, but both face difficulties.

The passage now turns to the philosophers' account of determination. They propose two main theories: one based on essence (leading to a single individual) and another based on matter individualized by accidents (allowing multiplicity). Both are critiqued, including an objection of circularity. The author also offers a response, suggesting an alternative view.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' two views
Determine the first view (essence) and second view (matter with accidents).
Notice how each view leads to a different conclusion about individuality.
2
Identify the objection
Find the circularity objection: accidents determine matter, yet accidents themselves require matter to be determined.
3
Understand the reply and counter-argument
The philosophers reply that accidents are determined by themselves. The author counters: then attributes can determine essence.
4
Compare with the Ash'ari view
Reflect on how this critique reinforces the Ash'ari position that determination requires divine will.
This is a key takeaway for the overall discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the unique combination of traits that make you who you are—your strengths, weaknesses, appearance, and personality—do you instinctively attribute them to random factors or to Allah's precise will? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly believed every detail was determined by Him?
?

Can you now see it as part of Allah's determination for you? What would it cost you to fully accept that nothing about you is accidental?
?
The author refutes the philosophers by arguing that if accidents can be self-determined, then essence can be determined by its own attributes. Do you ever claim ownership of your good qualities (e.g., 'I am naturally patient') without realizing they are given by Allah? Write down one attribute you are proud of and consciously attribute it to Allah's determination right now.

Answering the Objection that Existence Alone Determines Particularization
Response to the argument that circularity does not prove causality and that conceptual removal suffices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the constant conjunction of existence and particularization does not prove existence is the sole cause, and why the author's response demonstrates the need for additional factors.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction does not imply causation; existence alone cannot produce specific particularization.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical objection: that the observed correlation between existence and particularization does not necessarily mean existence causes particularization. The objector also claims that one can be certain of particularization just by considering existence, ignoring all other factors. The author’s rebuttal clarifies the logical limits of such claims and reinforces the need for a cause beyond mere existence to explain specific determinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening where the objector argues that dawran (constant conjunction) does not prove causality. Existence might merely accompany particularization, not cause it.
Notice the distinction between 'that with which' and 'that by which'.
2
Examine the sub-objection
The objector further claims that we can be certain of particularization solely from external existence, disregarding everything else.
This is a stronger claim that tries to isolate existence as the sole factor.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author replies that disregarding something does not negate it. Existence always comes with essence and causes. Moreover, even if existence were the cause, it would only produce a general determination, not specific ones.
The key point: specific particularization requires more than just existence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن الدوران لا يفيد العلية، فيجوز أن يكون الوجود ما معه التعين لا ما به التعين. فإن قيل: نحن نقطع بالتعين عند الوجود الخارجي، مع قطع النظر عن جميع ما عداه. قلنا: قطع النظر عن الشيء لا يوجب انتفاؤه، فعند الوجود لا بد من ماهية وأسباب فاعلية أو مادية. وبالجملة أمر يستند إليه الوجود، فيجوز أن يستند التشخص أيضاً إليه، ولو سلم فالوجود لا يقتضي إلا تعيناً ما، والكلام في التعينات المخصوصة، فلا يثبت المطلوب ما لم يتبين أن وجود كل فرد يقتضي تعينه الخاص.
And it was objected that circularity (al-dawran) does not indicate causality, so it is possible that existence is that with which particularization occurs, not that by which it occurs. For if it is said: We are certain of particularization upon external existence, disregarding everything other than it. We say: Disregarding something does not necessitate its non-existence. So upon existence, there must be an essence (mahiyya) and efficient or material causes. In sum, there is something to which existence is attributed, so it is possible that particularization (al-tashakhkhus) is also attributed to it. And even if that is granted, existence does not necessitate any particular determination except a general one, and the discussion is about specific determinations. So the desired conclusion is not established unless it is shown that the existence of each individual necessitates its specific determination.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage contains a third objection and its answer. The objector argues that the constant conjunction (dawran) between existence and particularization does not prove that existence is the cause of particularization; it could be that existence merely accompanies particularization. A further objection claims that one can be certain of particularization solely by considering external existence, while ignoring everything else. The author replies that ignoring something does not prove its non-existence. When existence occurs, essence and various causes (efficient or material) are still necessary. So the same ground that supports existence could also support particularization. Moreover, even if existence were the cause, it would only produce a general determination (some particularization), not the specific determination unique to each individual. Thus, the objector's point is insufficient to prove that existence alone is the cause of specific particularization.

Key Terms
دوران (dawran, circularity)
علية (illiyya, causality)
تعين (ta'ayyun, particularization)
تشخص (tashakhkhus)
ماهية (mahiyya, essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant conjunction (dawran) between existence and particularization is sufficient to prove that existence is the cause of particularization.
Correction: Dawran does not prove causality; it only shows correlation. The author argues that other factors (essence, causes) may be responsible, and existence alone does not produce specific particularization.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that disregarding other factors establishes the sufficiency of existence for particularization?
A) Because disregarding something proves its non-existence.
B) Because disregarding something does not prove its non-existence, so other factors may still be necessary.
C) Because existence is not present at all.
D) Because particularization is independent of existence.

Key Takeaway
Existence alone cannot cause specific particularization; additional causes such as essence and efficient/material causes are necessary.

How can you take greater responsibility for the specific determinations in your life starting today?

Philosophers on the Basis of Particularization
Exposition of the philosophers' view on the basis of particularization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' theory that particularization either stems from the essence itself (as in the Necessary Being) or from a material locus for contingent beings.

Core Idea
Particularization either comes from the essence or from matter.

The philosophers offer a systematic account of what makes an individual distinct. They argue that for the Necessary Being, particularization arises from its essence alone, while for contingent beings it must be traced to a material locus. This passage explains why alternative explanations—such as a separate cause or an inherent state—are impossible, and clarifies the role of matter in individuating bodies, souls, and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence: particularization can be attributed to the essence itself or its concomitants (as in the Necessary Being), or to something else.
Notice that the Necessary Being is given as a special case.
2
Understand why not separate or state
The passage rejects two possibilities: a separate cause and a state (hal). A separate cause would relate equally to all individuals; a state would be posterior and dependent.
The reasoning hinges on priority and dependency.
3
Recognize the three types of locus
The locus for an individual is its subject (for accidents), its matter (for bodies), or its relation (for souls). This is what particularizes contingent beings.
Each type corresponds to a different category of being.
4
Note the exception for immaterial intellects
Immaterial intellects are individuated by their essences alone, not by matter or relation. This is similar to the Necessary Being.
This shows that essence-based particularization is possible for non-material entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن التعين قد يستند إلى الماهية بنفسها أو بلوازمها كمها في الواجب. فينحصر في شخص، وإلا لزم تخلف المعلول عن علته لتحقق الماهية في كل فرد مع عدم تشخص الآخر، وقد يستند إلى غيرها، ولا يجوز أن يكون أمراً منفصلاً عن الشخص، لأن نسبته إلى كل الأفراد والتعينات على السواء، ولا حالاً فيه - لأن الحال في الشخص لافتقاره إليه يكون متأخراً عنه، ولكونه علة لتشخصه المتقدم عليه ضرورة أنه لا هذا الشخص إلا بهذا التشخص فيكون متقدماً عليه، وهو محال، فتعين أن يكون محلا له، وما ذكرنا من نسبة الحال والمحل إلى الشخص دون الماهية، أو التشخص أقرب وأوفق بكلامهم والمراد بمحل الشخص معروضه في الأعراض، ومادته في الأجسام، ومتعلقه في النفوس على ما ذكروه من حدوث النفس بعد المدد وتعينها به. فالعقول المجردة تستند تعيناتها إلى ماهياتها فينحصر كل في شخص لا إلى مجرد الإضافة كعقل الفلك الأول مثلاً على ما قيل لأن هذه الإضافة متأخرة عن وجود الفلك المتأخر عن وجود العقل وتعينه، والاستناد إلى المادة أعم من أن يكون بنفسها، أو بواسطة ما
And the philosophers held that particularization (al-ta'ayyun) may be attributed to the essence (al-mahiyya) itself or its concomitants, as in the case of the Necessary Being (al-wajib). So it is restricted to one individual; otherwise, the effect would lag behind its cause, since the essence would be realized in every individual without the particularization of the other. And it may be attributed to something else. And it is not permissible that it be something separate from the individual, because its relation to all individuals and particularizations is equal, nor a state (hal) in it — because the state in the individual, due to its need for it, would be posterior to it, and because it is the cause of its particularization which is prior to it, necessarily: for there is no this individual except by this particularization, so it would be prior to it, which is impossible. So it must be a locus (mahall) for it. And what we mentioned of the relation of the state and the locus to the individual rather than the essence, or the particularization, is closer and more consistent with their words. And what is meant by the locus of the individual is its subject in accidents, its matter in bodies, and its relation in souls, according to what they mentioned about the origination of the soul after [certain?] periods and its particularization by it. As for the immaterial intellects, their particularizations are attributed to their essences, so each is restricted to one individual, not to mere relation like the intellect of the first sphere, for example, as was said, because this relation is posterior to the existence of the sphere, which is posterior to the existence of the intellect and its particularization. And attribution to matter is more general, whether it be by itself or by means of something else.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' theory of particularization. They explain that particularization can originate from the essence itself (as in the Necessary Being, whose essence necessitates unity) or from something else (such as matter). For contingent beings, particularization cannot be a separate entity nor a state inhering in the individual, because a state would be posterior and dependent. Instead, it must be the locus itself: for accidents, it is the substance; for bodies, it is matter; for souls, it is the relation to the body. Immaterial intellects, however, are individuated by their essences alone, not by external relations. The passage concludes that attribution to matter is more general, covering both direct and indirect causation.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun, particularization)
ماهية (mahiyya, essence)
واجب (wajib, necessary)
حال (hal, state)
محل (mahall, locus)
مادة (madda, matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That particularization is always due to external factors; for necessary beings it is from the essence itself.
Correction: The philosophers distinguish: for the Necessary Being, particularization stems from its essence, while for contingent beings it is from a material locus.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the basis of particularization for the Necessary Being?
A) The matter.
B) The accident.
C) The essence itself or its concomitants.
D) A separate cause.

Key Takeaway
For the philosophers, particularization of the Necessary Being is from its essence, while for all other beings it must be traced to a material locus.

?
Your identity as a created being depends on matter and external factors beyond your control. In what ways do you still act as if your worth or existence comes from yourself? How can this lesson reshape your humility before God?
?
The philosophers argue that a 'state' (hal) cannot be the source of particularization because it is posterior and dependent. Consider your own fleeting traits and moods—do you let them define who you are? How can you anchor your identity in the unchanging reality of your relationship with God rather than in temporary states?

The Objection of Individuation and Its Answer
Objection and answer on individuation by matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that matter must already be individuated and the response that matter is intrinsically receptive to multiplicity through successive accidents.

Core Idea
Matter is intrinsically receptive to multiplicity; accidents provide individuation.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against attributing individuation to matter. The objector argues that matter itself would need to be individuated, leading to a trilemma. The response denies the objection by explaining that matter is not already individuated but becomes individuated through a succession of accidents, and matter is inherently receptive to multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and trilemma
Carefully read the first part of the passage where the objector presents three possibilities: essence, circularity, or infinite regress. Notice how each is argued to be impossible.
The trilemma is a classic argument structure.
2
Understand the response's key move
The response rejects the premise that matter is already individuated. Instead, matter receives accidents that change over time, allowing multiplicity without circularity or regress.
Accidents like quantities and qualities are the key.
3
Grasp the concept of receptivity
The response states that matter is receptive to multiplicity by itself—it does not need another receptacle, only an efficient cause to actualize the multiplicity.
This is the crucial point that avoids the trilemma.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the objection and answer fit into the broader discussion of individuation. Why is matter considered the principle of individuation in this tradition?
This passage defends the standard Ashʿarī view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما اعترض بأن المادة التي يستند إليها الشخص، تكون متشخصة لا محالة، فتشخصها إما لماهيتها فلا تتعدد أفرادها، أو للتشخص المعلول فيدور، أو لمادة أخرى فيتسلسل. أجيب بأنه لما فيها من الكميات والكيفيات، والأوضاع، وغير ذلك من الأعراض التي تتعاقب عليها بتعاقب الاستعدادات حتى لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية، لم يمتنع على ما هو رأيهم فيما لا يجتمع في الوجود، كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية. وإذا استند التشخص إلى المادة تكثرت أفراد الماهية بتكثر المواد والمادة قابلة للتكثر بذاتها فلا تفتقر إلى قابل آخر، وإنما تفتقر إلى فاعل يكثرها.
And when it was objected that the matter to which the individual is attributed is necessarily individuated, so its individuation is either by its essence, then its individuals would not be multiple; or by the effected individuation, then there is circularity; or by another matter, then there is infinite regress. It was answered: because of the quantities, qualities, positions, and other accidents that succeed upon it according to the succession of dispositions, even if they go on to infinity, it is not impossible—according to their view regarding what does not coexist in existence, like motions and celestial positions. And if individuation is attributed to matter, the individuals of the essence become multiple through the multiplicity of matters, and matter is receptive to multiplicity by itself, so it does not need another receptacle, but it only needs an agent to multiply it.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the principle of individuation. The objector argues: if individuation is attributed to matter, that matter itself must already be individuated. This leads to a trilemma: either matter's individuation is by its essence (then it cannot have multiple individuals), or by the very individuation it produces (circular), or by another matter (infinite regress). The response denies the objection. It explains that matter is not already individuated intrinsically; rather, it receives a succession of accidents (quantities, qualities, positions, etc.) that change as dispositions change. These accidents can be infinite in succession even if they never coexist, like successive motions. Therefore, matter itself is receptive to multiplicity by its very nature—it does not require an additional 'receptacle' for multiplicity, only an efficient cause to actualize the multiplicity. Thus, no vicious regress or circularity arises.

Key Terms
المادة (al-mādda) - matter
التشخص (al-tashakhkhuṣ) - individuation
الماهية (al-māhiyya) - essence
الكميات (al-kammiyyāt) - quantities
الكيفيات (al-kayfiyyāt) - qualities
الأعراض (al-aʿrāḍ) - accidents
الاستعدادات (al-istiʿdādāt) - dispositions
الفاعل (al-fāʿil) - efficient cause

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any infinite series of accidents is impossible, making the response invalid.
Correction: The response clarifies that successive non-coexisting series, like motions, are acceptable in the tradition, so the infinite series of accidents is not problematic.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason given in the response to avoid circularity or infinite regress?
A) Matter is individuated by its essence
B) Matter is receptive to multiplicity by itself
C) Accidents are eternal
D) The efficient cause is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Matter is intrinsically receptive to multiplicity, and the succession of accidents explains individuation without circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The trilemma shows that seeking the source of your identity in matter alone leads to circularity or infinity. Does your life reflect a trust in the Agent who gives you existence moment by moment?
?
Matter needs an agent to multiply it. Are you living as though you are self-sufficient, or are you consciously dependent on the Creator for your very individuation?

Individuation of Matter: Objection and Answer
First objection and answer on matter's individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that matter must already be individuated, leading to a trilemma, and the author's defense that matter can multiply by itself through successive accidents.

Core Idea
Matter is multiplicable by itself, requiring only an agent, not another substrate.

In this passage, the author addresses a fundamental objection to using accidents as the principle of individuation: matter itself appears to already be individuated. If matter is individuated by its essence, then it cannot have multiple individuals; if by an effect, circularity; if by another matter, infinite regress. The author responds by emphasizing that matter contains a succession of accidents and is multiplicable by itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trilemma
The objection presents three possibilities for matter's individuation: by essence, by an effect (circular), or by another matter (infinite regress). Understand each horn and why each is problematic.
This trilemma is the core of the objection.
2
Examine the answer
The author argues that matter's accidents (quantities, qualities, positions) succeed each other indefinitely, and that matter is inherently multiplicable. It does not require a recipient substrate for its multiplicity, only an agent to multiply it.
Matter's capacity for successive accidents avoids infinite regress.
3
Reflect on the agent
Note the key distinction: matter does not need a further matter (recipient) to become multiple, but it does need an efficient cause (agent) to actualize its multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يرد ما قيل : أن غير المنفصل لا ينحصر فيما يكون في التشخص أو محلاً له، لجواز أن يكون حالاً في محله. ولما اعترض بأن المادة التي يستند إليها الشخص، تكون متشخصة لا محالة، فتشخصها إما لماهيتها فلا تتعدد أفرادها، أو للتشخص المعلول فيدور، أو لمادة أخرى فيتسلسل . أجيب بأنه لما فيها من الكميات والكيفيات، والأوضاع، وغير ذلك من الأعراض التي تتعاقب عليها بتعاقب الاستعدادات حتى لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية، لم يمتنع على ما هو رأيهم فيما لا يجتمع في الوجود، كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية. وإذا استند التشخص إلى المادة تكثرت أفراد الماهية بتكثر المواد والمادة قابلة للتكثر بذاتها فلا تفتقر إلى قابل آخر، وإنما تفتقر إلى فاعل يكثرها . واعترض على ما ذكروا بعد تسليم مقدماته بأن تعين الأعراض الحالة التي في المادة إنما هو بتعين المادة على ما سيجيء، فلو تعينت المادة بها كان دوراً. وأجيب بأن تعين المادة إنما هو بنفس الأعراض الحالة في المادة المعينة بتعين ما لا بتعيناتها الحاصلة بتعين المادة، وحاصله أن تعيناتها بتعينها وتعينها مع تعيناتها، فلا يلزم الدور، ولا حصول التشخص من انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي. إلا أنه يرد عليه أنه إذا جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز تكثر الماهية وتعين أفرادها بما لها من الصفات المتكثرة العارضة لها من غير لزوم مادة .
So it is not refuted what was said: that the non-separate (accident) is not confined to being either in the individuation or in a locus for it, because it is possible for it to be inherent in its locus. And when it was objected that the matter upon which the individual depends is necessarily individuated, so its individuation either is due to its essence, then its individuals would not be multiple, or due to the individuation that is an effect, leading to circularity, or due to another matter, leading to infinite regress. It was answered: because of what it contains of quantities, qualities, positions, and other accidents that succeed one another according to the succession of dispositions, such that if they went on to infinity, it would not be impossible according to their view regarding things that do not coexist in existence, like celestial motions and positions. And if individuation is based on matter, the individuals of the essence multiply by the multiplication of matters, and matter is multiplicable by itself, so it does not need another recipient; rather, it needs an agent to multiply it. And an objection was raised against what they mentioned after conceding its premises: that the determination of the inhering accidents in matter is only by the determination of matter, as will come, so if matter is determined by them, there would be circularity. And it was answered: that the determination of matter is only by the accidents themselves inhering in the matter determined by a determination other than their own determinations that result from the determination of matter. The gist is that their determinations are by its determination, and its determination is with their determinations, so circularity does not follow, nor does the occurrence of individuation from the conjunction of two universals. However, it is objected against this: if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible for the essence to multiply and its individuals to be determined by its multiple attributes that are accidental to it without requiring matter?
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and answers concerning the theory of individuation (tashakhkhus) of material individuals. The author defends the view that accidents (a'rad) such as quantities, qualities, and positions can serve as the principle of individuation for matter, without leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The first objection claims that matter itself is necessarily individuated, so its individuation either comes from its essence (then no multiplicity), from the effect (circular), or from another matter (infinite regress). The answer explains that matter contains a succession of accidents that can go to infinity without incoherence, and that matter is multiplicable by itself, needing only an agent to multiply it. A second objection argues that accidents themselves are determined by matter, so if matter is determined by them, circularity results. The answer holds that the determination of matter is by the accidents in a way that does not presuppose their own determination from matter, thus avoiding circularity. Finally, a further objection questions: if accidents can individuate, why could essence not be individuated by its own multiple accidental attributes without needing matter? This final objection is left unanswered in the passage, indicating a point for further discussion.

المادة
العرض
التعين
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter cannot be multiplied because it is already individuated; any multiplication would require another substrate.
Correction: Matter is multiplicable by itself; it does not need another matter to receive multiplicity, only an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what is the problem if matter is individuated by its essence?
A) It would lead to infinite regress.
B) Its individuals would not be multiple.
C) It would cause circularity.
D) It would require an external agent.

Key Takeaway
Matter is multiplicable by itself, so the trilemma about its individuation is resolved by appealing to its inherent potentiality for multiplicity and a series of accidents.

Resolving Circularity Between Matter and Accidents
Second objection and answer on circularity, plus final objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that accidents are determined by matter, causing circularity if matter is determined by them, and the author's resolution through a distinction in modes of determination.

Core Idea
The determination of matter and accidents is interdependent but non-circular.

A second objection argues that accidents themselves are determined by matter, so if matter is determined by these accidents, we have circularity. The author answers by clarifying that the determination of matter is by the accidents themselves, not by their own determinations that come from matter. This avoids the circle. However, a final objection is raised: if accidents can individuate, why not attributes without matter?

Guided Path
1
Understand the circularity objection
The objector concedes the premises so far but argues: accidents are determined by matter, so if matter is determined by them, circularity results.
This is a classic dawr (circularity) argument.
2
Analyze the author's distinction
The author distinguishes between two senses: the determination of matter is by the accidents themselves (which are already determined with a different determination), not by the determinations that the accidents receive from matter. This avoids explicit circularity.
The gist: the accidents' individual determinations come from matter, but the matter's determination comes from the accidents as such.
3
Consider the final objection
The final question challenges the need for matter at all: if accidents can individuate, why can't essence be individuated by its own accidental attributes without a substrate? This is left unanswered, stimulating further reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يرد ما قيل : أن غير المنفصل لا ينحصر فيما يكون في التشخص أو محلاً له، لجواز أن يكون حالاً في محله. ولما اعترض بأن المادة التي يستند إليها الشخص، تكون متشخصة لا محالة، فتشخصها إما لماهيتها فلا تتعدد أفرادها، أو للتشخص المعلول فيدور، أو لمادة أخرى فيتسلسل . أجيب بأنه لما فيها من الكميات والكيفيات، والأوضاع، وغير ذلك من الأعراض التي تتعاقب عليها بتعاقب الاستعدادات حتى لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية، لم يمتنع على ما هو رأيهم فيما لا يجتمع في الوجود، كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية. وإذا استند التشخص إلى المادة تكثرت أفراد الماهية بتكثر المواد والمادة قابلة للتكثر بذاتها فلا تفتقر إلى قابل آخر، وإنما تفتقر إلى فاعل يكثرها . واعترض على ما ذكروا بعد تسليم مقدماته بأن تعين الأعراض الحالة التي في المادة إنما هو بتعين المادة على ما سيجيء، فلو تعينت المادة بها كان دوراً. وأجيب بأن تعين المادة إنما هو بنفس الأعراض الحالة في المادة المعينة بتعين ما لا بتعيناتها الحاصلة بتعين المادة، وحاصله أن تعيناتها بتعينها وتعينها مع تعيناتها، فلا يلزم الدور، ولا حصول التشخص من انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي. إلا أنه يرد عليه أنه إذا جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز تكثر الماهية وتعين أفرادها بما لها من الصفات المتكثرة العارضة لها من غير لزوم مادة .
So it is not refuted what was said: that the non-separate (accident) is not confined to being either in the individuation or in a locus for it, because it is possible for it to be inherent in its locus. And when it was objected that the matter upon which the individual depends is necessarily individuated, so its individuation either is due to its essence, then its individuals would not be multiple, or due to the individuation that is an effect, leading to circularity, or due to another matter, leading to infinite regress. It was answered: because of what it contains of quantities, qualities, positions, and other accidents that succeed one another according to the succession of dispositions, such that if they went on to infinity, it would not be impossible according to their view regarding things that do not coexist in existence, like celestial motions and positions. And if individuation is based on matter, the individuals of the essence multiply by the multiplication of matters, and matter is multiplicable by itself, so it does not need another recipient; rather, it needs an agent to multiply it. And an objection was raised against what they mentioned after conceding its premises: that the determination of the inhering accidents in matter is only by the determination of matter, as will come, so if matter is determined by them, there would be circularity. And it was answered: that the determination of matter is only by the accidents themselves inhering in the matter determined by a determination other than their own determinations that result from the determination of matter. The gist is that their determinations are by its determination, and its determination is with their determinations, so circularity does not follow, nor does the occurrence of individuation from the conjunction of two universals. However, it is objected against this: if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible for the essence to multiply and its individuals to be determined by its multiple attributes that are accidental to it without requiring matter?
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and answers concerning the theory of individuation (tashakhkhus) of material individuals. The author defends the view that accidents (a'rad) such as quantities, qualities, and positions can serve as the principle of individuation for matter, without leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The first objection claims that matter itself is necessarily individuated, so its individuation either comes from its essence (then no multiplicity), from the effect (circular), or from another matter (infinite regress). The answer explains that matter contains a succession of accidents that can go to infinity without incoherence, and that matter is multiplicable by itself, needing only an agent to multiply it. A second objection argues that accidents themselves are determined by matter, so if matter is determined by them, circularity results. The answer holds that the determination of matter is by the accidents in a way that does not presuppose their own determination from matter, thus avoiding circularity. Finally, a further objection questions: if accidents can individuate, why could essence not be individuated by its own multiple accidental attributes without needing matter? This final objection is left unanswered in the passage, indicating a point for further discussion.

Key Terms
تعين
الأعراض
الدور
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If accidents determine matter and matter determines accidents, that is a vicious circle.
Correction: The author shows that the two determinations are different: matter's determination is by the accidents as such, while accidents' particular determinations are by matter. This is not circular.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid circularity between matter and accidents?
A) By denying that accidents exist.
B) By claiming matter is determined by the accidents themselves, not by their own determinations from matter.
C) By asserting that accidents are eternal.
D) By saying matter needs no determination.

Key Takeaway
The mutual determination of matter and accidents is not circular when properly distinguished, but the possibility of individuation without matter remains an open question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your own thinking about your identity, do you confuse the essence of who you are with the accidents of your circumstances? This lesson challenges you to examine whether you treat your character as merely derived from external conditions or as something that defines those conditions.
?
The final objection asks why an essence cannot be individuated by its own attributes without matter. This implies that you might be over-relying on physical, material anchors to define yourself. Is your sense of self tied more to your body and possessions than to your inner, non-material essence? What would remain of 'you' if all material references were stripped away? Are you ready to face that possibility?
?
The author distinguishes between two kinds of determination to avoid circularity. When you face conflicting priorities or dependencies in your life, do you reflexively see them as paradoxes, or do you pause to distinguish the different roles they play? This lesson calls you to develop precision in your reasoning—not just in theology, but in how you understand your own heart's allegiances. Will you take the time to untangle your own circular thinking?

Alternative Argument: Denying Necessity
Sanusi presents an alternative proof from those who do not concede the necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative Ash'ari proof that does not rely on necessity to deny the affirmation of the non-existent.

Core Idea
If the non-existent were already real, divine power could not create.

Sanusi presents a second line of argument for those who do not accept the necessity of denying the affirmation of the non-existent. This alternative proof shows that if the non-existent were already affirmed in its essence, divine power would be unable to bring anything into existence. This reinforces the same conclusion through a different route.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's structure.
The Arabic is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Identify the 'if-then' structure: if the non-existent were affirmed, then divine power would be impossible. Ask yourself why this follows.
This argument does not depend on necessity, but on a logical consequence.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this proof strengthens the Ash'ari position against those who might claim the non-existent has some form of existence.
This is an alternative to the earlier necessity argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منا من جعل نفي ثبوت المعدوم غير ضروري فاستدل عليه بوجوده الأول: لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً لامتنع تأثير القدرة في شيء من الممكنات
Among us is one who made the negation of the affirmation of the non-existent not necessary, so he adduced for it with his first proof: if the non-existent were affirmed, then the effect of power would be impossible in any of the possibles.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This lesson presents an alternative line of argument from those Ash'aris who do not accept that the denial of the non-existent's existence is a matter of necessity. They instead provide a proof: If the non-existent were already affirmed (thabit) in its essence, then divine power could not bring it into existence, because power would be affecting something that already exists. This is a separate proof that does not rely on necessity. The text breaks off after stating this proof, but it is a complete argument in itself.

Key Takeaway

Preponderance Without a Cause: Free Choice vs. Necessity
Author distinguishes two types of preponderance without cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between the preponderance of a possible without a cause (impossible) and a free agent choosing between equals without a cause (not impossible).

Core Idea
Free choice can break equality without a cause; the possible cannot preponderate without a cause.

This passage clarifies a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: while it is impossible for a possible being to preponderate without a cause, a free agent (like God) can choose between equals without an external preponderator. This distinction underpins Ash'ari beliefs about God's creation at a specific time and His specification of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two cases the author distinguishes.
2
Identify the two cases
Identify the case of 'the possible preponderating without a cause' and 'a free agent choosing one of two equals without a preponderator'. Note that the author says the latter is not considered impossible.
3
Understand the examples and refutation
Examine the examples: God's creation at a specific time, specific rulings, and power to act/refrain. Also note how the author refutes the philosophers who deny choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يتأتى منه النظر والاستدلال، ثم اختلاف البعض في نفس الحكم أو في بداهته، والتفاوت بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين لا ينافي البداهة على ما سبق، وأما ما ذهب إليه الكثيرون من أن الله تعالى خلق العالم في وقت دون سائر الأوقات من غير مرجح ، وخصص أفعال المكلفين بأحكام مخصوصة من غير أن يكون فيها ما يقتضي ذلك، وأن قدرة القادر قد تتعلق بالفعل أو الترك من غير مرجح ، فليس من ترجح الممكن بلا مرجح، بل من ترجح المختار أحد المتساويين من غير مرجح ، ونحن لا نقول بامتناعه فضلاً عن أن يكون ضرورياً، وإلى هذا يستند عندنا اختلاف حركات الكواكب، ومواضعها، وأوضاعها. وأما الفلاسفة القائلون بالإيجاب دون الاختيار فلا يلتزمون وقوع تلك الاختلافات والاختصاصات بلا سبب، بل يعترفون باستنادها إلى أسباب فاعلية، لا اطلاع على تفاصيلها. ففي الجملة لم يقل أحد ممن يعتد به بوقوع الممكن بلا سبب .
Speculation and inference cannot be obtained from it, then some differ about the judgement itself or its self-evidence, and the discrepancy between it and our statement 'one is half of two' does not contradict self-evidence as previously. As for what many have held that God created the world at a time without other times without a preponderator, and specified the actions of the morally responsible with particular rulings without there being in them what necessitates that, and that the power of the powerful may attach to action or refraining without a preponderator – that is not from the preponderance of the possible without a preponderator, but rather from the preponderance of the free agent of one of two equals without a preponderator. And we do not say it is impossible, let alone that it is necessary. And upon this depends in our view the differences of the movements of the planets, their positions, and their states. As for the philosophers who assert necessitation without choice, they do not commit to the occurrence of those differences and particularizations without a cause, but rather acknowledge their dependence on efficient causes, with no knowledge of their details. In summary, no one who is considered has said that the possible occurs without a cause.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage defines the author's position on preponderance without a cause. It first clarifies that the discussion does not yield valid speculation or inference. Then it distinguishes between two cases: (1) the possible preponderating without a cause – which is impossible, and (2) a free agent choosing one of two equals without a preponderator – which the author says they do not hold to be impossible, let alone necessary. The author gives examples: God's creation at a specific time, specific rulings for actions, power attaching to act or refrain without a cause. These are instances of a free agent's choice, not of the possible preponderating without a cause. The author also mentions that planetary motions depend on this free choice. Finally, the philosophers who believe in necessary emanation (ijab) do not accept occurrence without a cause either; they attribute everything to efficient causes, even if unknown. So no reputable scholar says the possible occurs without a cause.

Key Terms

مرجح
ممكن
مختار
إيجاب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any occurrence without a cause is impossible, including God's free choice.
Correction: The author clarifies that a free agent (like God) can choose between equals without a cause; this is not the same as a possible preponderating without a cause. The latter is impossible, the former is not.

Checkpoint
What is the author's position on the possibility of a free agent choosing one of two equals without a preponderator?
A) It is impossible
B) It is necessary
C) They do not say it is impossible
D) It is only possible for God

Key Takeaway
A free agent's choice between equals without a cause is not impossible, unlike the preponderance of a possible without a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Weakness of the Proof for Causality
The author states and critiques a twofold proof for causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the standard proof for the possible's need of a cause is considered weak by the author.

Core Idea
The proof for causality has two weak steps.

The author now turns to a critical evaluation of the standard argument for why a possible thing requires a cause. He presents two lines of reasoning commonly used, but immediately declares them weak. This lesson examines both steps and sets the stage for a deeper critique of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the proof, then pronounces it weak.
The passage is dense; focus on the two claims being made.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
The proof has two parts. The first involves preponderance without a cause contradicting equality. The second involves a preponderator before existence as an existential attribute in the cause. List them separately.
3
Understand why each part is weak
Ask yourself: Why would each line of reasoning be considered weak? The author does not elaborate here, but think about potential objections. The commentary suggests both are problematic.
This prepares you for later critiques.
4
Consider the broader significance
Reflect on why this matters for kalam. If the proof for causality is weak, how do we establish that the world needs a Creator? This lesson highlights a problem that later discussions will address.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال على احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر قال: والاستدلال بأن وقوع أحدهما بلا سبب يقتضي رجحانه فينافي التساوي، وبأنه لا بد من مرجح قبل الوجود، وهو وجودي يقوم بالمؤثر ضرورة تأخر الأثر ضعيف.
The proof of the possible's need for a cause. He said: And the proof [based] on the occurrence of one of them without a cause necessitating its preponderance contradicts equality, and on [the claim] that there must be a preponderator prior to existence, and it is an existential [attribute] subsisting in the cause due to the necessary posteriority of the effect, is weak.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents a twofold proof for the possible's need of a cause and immediately pronounces it weak. The first part argues that if one of two equal possibilities occurs without a cause, that would necessitate its preponderance, contradicting their equality. The second part argues that before existence, there must be a preponderator (murajjih), which is an existential attribute that subsists in the cause, because the effect is necessarily posterior. The author considers both lines of reasoning weak, though he does not elaborate here why. The passage thus highlights that the standard argument for causality (ihtiyaj al-mumkin ila al-mu'aththir) is not decisive in the author's view.

Key Terms
استدلال
مؤثر
أثر
مرجح
ترجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that the proof for the possible's need of a cause is obviously correct and uncontroversial in kalam.
Correction: The author considers this proof weak, showing that even standard arguments for causality are subject to critique within the tradition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the proof for the possible's need of a cause weak?
A) Because it assumes preponderance before existence
B) Because it relies on an existential attribute in the cause
C) Because it contradicts equality
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The standard proof for the possible's need of a cause is not accepted as decisive; it is considered weak by the author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author leaves the proof's weakness unaddressed. If a familiar argument for God's existence were to fail, would your faith waver? Examine your heart: is your trust in God or in your ability to prove Him?
?
You have just read that a classic argument for the need of a cause is weak. Does this challenge your confidence in reason alone to reach theological truths? Will you continue to seek proofs, or will you turn more to revelation and spiritual certainty? Make a concrete decision today.
