Objection and Answer: The Necessity of a Preponderant Factor for Equal Alternatives
Presents and refutes the objection that contingent events can occur without a cause, using the example of two equal options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologian defends the principle of causality against the objection from equal alternatives by appealing to the will as a preponderant factor.

Core Idea
Equal alternatives do not disprove causality; the will itself is the preponderant factor.

One of the strongest objections against the necessity of a cause for every contingent event is drawn from everyday experience: when faced with two equal options, we choose one without any apparent reason. In this passage, the author presents this objection and offers a precise answer. He distinguishes between the occurrence of a possible without any cause and the choice of an agent between equals, pointing to the will as the true cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and examples
Read the objection carefully: if no cause were needed, then equal choices would be impossible, yet they occur. Note the examples: thirsty person, hungry person, fleeing person.
The examples are meant to demonstrate an apparent counterexample to the causal principle.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The author concedes that there is no external preponderant factor in these cases, but he redefines what is at stake. Focus on the distinction between 'occurrence of a possible without cause' versus 'choice of an agent between equals'.
This distinction is crucial: the objection conflates two different things.
3
Grasp the second part: the will as cause
A further objection says: the choice itself is a possible without cause. The author denies this, stating that the choice occurs by the will, which inherently has the property of preponderance and specification.
The will is not an external cause but the internal cause that breaks the symmetry.
4
Reflect on the overall defense of causality
Consider how this answer preserves the principle that every contingent event has a cause. The will is a real cause even if not external. Ask yourself: does this answer fully resolve the objection?
This is a classic Ash'ari argument that upholds causality while acknowledging human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه لو امتنع وقوع الممكن بلا مؤثر وترجحه بلا مرجح لما وقع. واللازم باطل بحكم الضرورة، في مثل العطشان يشرب أحد الماءين، والجائع يأكل أحد الرغيفين، والهارب من السبع يسلك أحد الطريقين مع فرض التساوي وعدم المرجح. والجواب بعد تسليم عدم المرجح عند العقل أصلاً، أن هذا ليس من وقوع الممكن بلا سبب وترجح أحد طرفيه بلا مرجح، بل من ترجيح المختار أحد الأمرين المتساويين من غير مرجح ومخصص، وهو غير المتنازع. فإن قيل هذا الاختيار والترجيح أمر ممكن وقع بلا سبب وفيه المطلوب. قلنا ممنوع بل إنما وقع بالإرادة التي من شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص.
And among them is that if the occurrence of the possible without an agent and its preponderance without a preponderant factor were impossible, it would not occur. But the consequent is false by necessity, as in the case of a thirsty person drinking one of two waters, a hungry person eating one of two loaves, and a person fleeing from a beast taking one of two paths, assuming equality and absence of a preponderant factor. The answer: after conceding that there is no preponderant factor in the intellect at all, this is not an occurrence of the possible without a cause and the preponderance of one of its two sides without a preponderant factor, but rather from the choice of the agent between two equal things without a preponderant factor and specifier, and this is not the point of dispute. For if it is said: this choice and preponderance is a possible occurrence without a cause, and the required conclusion is present. We say: it is denied; rather it occurred by the will whose property is preponderance and specification.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states: If it were impossible for a contingent (mumkin) to occur without an agent (mu'aththir) and for one of its two sides to become preponderant without a preponderant factor (murajjih), then it would never occur. But the consequent is false by necessity, as seen in everyday examples: a thirsty person chooses one of two equal cups of water, a hungry person chooses one of two equal loaves of bread, a person fleeing a beast chooses one of two equal paths—all without an apparent preponderant factor. Therefore, the initial claim is false. The answer begins by conceding that there is no preponderant factor in the intellect in these cases. However, this is not a case of a contingent occurring without a cause; rather, it is a case of an agent's choice (tarjīḥ al-mukhtār) between two equal things without an external preponderant factor or specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). This is not the point of dispute. A further objection is raised: this choice and preponderance (ikhtiyār wa tarjīḥ) is itself a possible occurrence without a cause, which would prove the desired conclusion. The answer is that this is denied; rather, the choice occurs by the will (irādah) whose property is to preponderate and specify. Thus, the objection fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equal alternatives prove that contingent events can occur without any cause.
Correction: The choice between equals is caused by the agent's will, which is a preponderant factor internal to the agent, thus preserving the causal principle.

Checkpoint

C) By claiming that the options are not truly equal

The choice between equals is caused by the agent's will, preserving the principle that every contingent requires a cause.

Why Non-Existence Does Not Need a Cause
Refutes the claim that non-existence requires a cause, distinguishing between pure negation and attributed non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that non-existence would also need a cause is false, and how non-existence is a pure negation yet relatable to possible beings.

Core Idea
Non-existence needs no cause because it is a pure negation, but when attributed to a possible it depends on the absence of a cause.

This lesson addresses a key objection in arguments about causality: if existence needs a cause, why wouldn't non-existence? The scholar clarifies that non-existence, being a pure negation, cannot be an effect; yet it has a relational aspect that explains its dependence without requiring an active agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Start by reading the objection: if a possible needs an agent for its existence, it would also need one for its non-existence because existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the possible. Notice the claim that non-existence is a pure negation and thus cannot be an effect.
The objection relies on the equality of existence and non-existence in relation to the possible.
2
Follow the two-part answer
The answer begins by denying the implication (mulāzamah) if non-existence cannot be an effect. Then it presents a second scenario: if non-existence were suitable as an effect, the falsity of the consequent is denied. Trace how each part undermines the objection.
The answer uses a conditional structure: 'if ... then ...' with a denial.
3
Understand the verification of non-existence
The author provides a verification: non-existence is not a pure negation in every sense; it is attributed to a possible existent, so it depends on the absence of the cause of its existence. This relational aspect explains why non-existence is different from existence.
This verification resolves the apparent parity between existence and non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه لو احتاج الممكن في وجوده إلى المؤثر، لاحتاج إليه في عدمه لتساويها واللازم باطل لأن العدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً. والجواب أن العدم إن لم يصلح أثراً، منعنا الملازمة لجواز أن يتساوى الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن لكن لا يحتاج العدم إلى المؤثر لعدم صلوحه لذلك بخلاف الوجود، فإن المقتضى فيه سالم عن المانع، وإن صلح أثراً منعنا بطلان اللازم، وهو ظاهر وتحقيقه أنه وإن كان نفياً صرفاً، بمعنى أنه ليس له شائبة الوجود العيني، لكن ليس نفياً صرفاً. بمعنى أن لا يضاف إلى ما يتصف بالوجود، بل هو عدم مضاف إلى الممكن الوجود فيستند إلى عدم علة وجوده بمعنى احتياجه إليه عند العقل، حيث يحكم بأنه إنما بقى عدمه الأصلي، أو اتصف بعدمه الطارئ، بناء على عدم وجوده مستمراً أو طارئاً.
And among them is that if the possible needed the agent for its existence, it would need it for its non-existence, due to their equality; and the consequent is false because non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be an effect. The answer: if non-existence cannot be an effect, we deny the implication because it is possible that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of the possible, but non-existence does not need the agent due to its not being suitable for that, unlike existence, for the requisite in it is free of the impediment. And if it is suitable as an effect, we deny the falsity of the consequent, and it is clear. Its verification is that it, even if it is a pure negation in the sense that it has no trace of concrete existence, it is not a pure negation in the sense that it is not attributed to what is characterized by existence; rather it is a non-existence attributed to the possible of existence, so it is based on the non-existence of the cause of its existence in the sense of its need for it in the intellect, where it judges that it either remained in its original non-existence or became characterized by an accidental non-existence, based on the non-existence of its existence continuously or accidentally.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objection: if a possible (mumkin) needs an agent (mu'aththir) for its existence, it would need one for its non-existence as well, because existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the possible. But the consequent is false, since non-existence (ʻadam) is a pure negation and cannot be an effect (athar). The answer begins by denying the implication (mulāzamah) if non-existence cannot be an effect, because existence and non-existence can be equal in terms of the essence of the possible, but non-existence does not need an agent because it is not suitable for that, unlike existence where the requisite (muqtaḍā) is free of impediment. If non-existence were considered suitable as an effect, then the falsity of the consequent is denied. The author then provides a verification (taḥqīq): non-existence, though a pure negation in that it has no concrete existence, is not a pure negation in the sense that it is not attributed to something that is characterized by existence. Rather, it is a non-existence attributed to a possible existent (mumkin al-wujūd), so it depends on the absence of the cause of its existence, in the sense that the intellect judges that it either remained in its original non-existence or acquired an accidental non-existence, based on the absence of its existence continuously or accidentally. Thus, non-existence does have a relational aspect that distinguishes it, but it does not require an efficient cause.

Misconception: Since existence requires a cause, non-existence must also require one.
Correction: Non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be an effect; it depends on the absence of a cause, not on an active agent.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be an effect; its attribution to a possible does not imply a cause but rather the absence of a cause.

Non-Existence as a Cause: Objection and Answer
Refutes the argument that non-existence cannot be a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the role of non-existence as a cause in intellectual reasoning while denying its applicability to external causality.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a cause in the mind, but not in external reality.

This lesson tackles a key objection against the idea that non-existence can serve as a cause. The objection claims that non-existence lacks distinction and that a cause must be existent. The answer clarifies the role of intellectual judgment and distinguishes between mental and external causality, preserving the proof of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the passage from 'فإن قيل' to 'معلولاً'. Understand the two arguments: (1) a cause must be existent, (2) non-existences are indistinguishable.
The objection is a common challenge in kalām discussions on causality.
2
Analyze the answer's first part
Read from 'قلنا' to 'ممنوع'. Notice how the author denies that the universal concept of non-existence is purely non-existent and that the lack of distinction is denied.
The initial refutation targets the premises of the objection.
3
Understand the verification
Read from 'التحقيق' to 'حكم العقل'. The author explains that non-existences can be distinct in the intellect, so one can be a cause for another in mental reasoning.
Key point: intellectual causality does not require external existence.
4
Grasp the theological safeguard
Read 'ولا يلزم عنه' to the end. The author clarifies that intellectual causality does not imply external causality, thus the proof of God remains valid.
This prevents the objection from leading to atheism.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Summarize the difference between intellectual and external causality. Why is this distinction important for theology?
It allows rational arguments without committing to physical causation by non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل العدم لا يصلح علة، لأن العلة وجودية، لكونها نقيض اللاعلية العدمية، فيفتقر إلى موصوف وجودي، ولأنه لا تمايز في الإعدام فلا يصلح بعضها علة، وبعضها معلولاً. قلنا مجرد صورة السلب أو الصدق على المعدوم في الجملة لا تقتضي كون المفهوم الكلي عدمياً، بجميع جزئياته، ولو سلم فنقيض العدمي لا يلزم أن يكون وجودياً. وقد سبق مثل ذلك، وعدم تمايز الإعدام ممنوع. التحقيق أن تساوي طرفي الممكن إلا في العقل، فالمرجح لا يكون إلا وعدم العلة، أو عدم الممكن ليس نفياً صرفاً، بل كل منهما ثابت في العقل ممتاز عن الآخر فيصلح أحدهما علة للآخر في حكم العقل، ولا يلزم عنه صلوح عليته للوجود ليلزم انسداد إثبات الصانع، لأن ذلك إنما يكون بحسب الخارج.
For if it is said: non-existence is not fit to be a cause, because the cause is existent, being the contrary of non-existent causelessness, so it needs an existent subject, and because there is no distinction among non-existences, so some of them cannot be a cause and others an effect. We say: the mere form of negation or truthfulness about the non-existent in general does not entail that the universal concept is non-existent in all its particulars; and if it is conceded, the contrary of the non-existent is not necessarily existent. And the like has preceded. And the lack of distinction among non-existences is denied. The verification: that the equality of the two sides of the possible exists only in the intellect, so the preponderant factor is nothing but the absence of the cause, or the non-existence of the possible is not a pure negation; rather each of them is established in the intellect distinct from the other, so one of them is fit to be a cause for the other in the judgment of the intellect, and its being fit as a cause for existence does not follow from it, which would entail the closure of proving the Maker, because that is only with respect to the external.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objection argues that non-existence (ʻadam) cannot be a cause (ʻillah) because a cause must be existent (wujūdī), being the opposite of non-existent causelessness (al-lāʻilliyyah al-ʻadamiyyah), and therefore requires an existent subject. Additionally, there is no distinction among non-existences (lā tamyīz fī al-īʻdām), so some cannot serve as a cause and others as an effect. The answer begins by stating that the mere form of negation or truth about the non-existent in general does not entail that the universal concept is non-existent in all its particulars. Even if conceded, the contrary of the non-existent is not necessarily existent. The lack of distinction among non-existences is denied. The verification (taḥqīq) explains: the equality of the two sides of the possible exists only in the intellect, so the preponderant factor (murajjih) is nothing but the absence of the cause, or the non-existence of the possible is not a pure negation. Rather, each non-existence is established in the intellect as distinct from the other, so one of them can be a cause for the other in the judgment of the intellect. From this, it does not follow that it is fit as a cause for existence in the external world, which would close the door to proving the Creator; because such fitness pertains only to the external realm.

Correction: The author clarifies that intellectual causality does not entail external causality; the proof of the Creator remains valid because external causality requires existence.

Key Takeaway

The Objection on the Loci of Influence
Presents the objection against the three possible loci of influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an agent's influence on a possible cannot affect quiddity, existence, or qualification, and why this challenges the need for a cause.

Core Idea
The possible's quiddity, existence, and qualification are invariant—so what does a cause actually do?

In this passage, a serious objection is raised against the causal argument for the existence of God. It argues that if a possible being needs a cause, the cause's effect must be on either the quiddity, existence, or qualification of that possible—but none of these can be truly affected. Understanding this objection is crucial for engaging with the broader debate on causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the precise phrasing of the three possibilities.
2
Identify the three loci
The objection lists three possible targets of an agent's influence: quiddity (essence), existence, and being qualified by existence. Underline them in the translation.
These are the only conceivable loci.
3
Understand the invalidity claim
The objection claims all three are invalid, referencing earlier arguments about the thingness of the non-existent and the made-ness of quiddity. Ask yourself: why would quiddity be unaffected by an agent?
This references prior material not included here.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider the implications: if none of these can be influenced, then what does a cause actually do? This objection undermines the proof that every possible needs a cause.
The answer to this objection is not provided in this lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الممكن لو احتاج إلى مؤثر، فتأثيره إما في ماهية الممكن أو وجوده أو موصوفيته بالوجود، إذ لا يعقل غير ذلك، والكل باطل لما مر في نفي شيئية المعدوم، ومجعولية الماهية من أن الماهية ماهية والوجود وجود والموصوفية موصوفية سواء وجد الغير أو لم يوجد.
And among them is that if the possible needed an agent, then its influence is either on the quiddity of the possible, or its existence, or its being qualified by existence; nothing else is conceivable. And all are invalid, due to what has passed regarding the denial of the thingness of the non-existent and the made-ness of quiddity: that quiddity is quiddity, existence is existence, and being qualified is being qualified, whether the other exists or not.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection against the necessity of a cause for the possible. The objection states: If the possible (mumkin) needs an agent (mu'aththir), then the agent's influence must be either on the quiddity (māhiyyah) of the possible, or on its existence (wujūd), or on its being qualified by existence (mawṣūfiyyah bi al-wujūd). No other possibility is conceivable. However, all three are invalid, based on previous arguments that denied the 'thingness' (shay'iyyah) of the non-existent and the 'made-ness' (maj'ūliyyah) of quiddity: quiddity remains quiddity, existence remains existence, and being qualified remains being qualified, whether the other (the agent) exists or not. This objection references earlier discussions (presumably from the same work) to challenge the need for a cause. The answer to this objection is not included in the present window, as the text ends here. The passage thus presents a significant challenge to the causal proof for the existence of God.

الماهية
الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the influence of a cause must affect something real; but this objection argues that quiddity, existence, and qualification are invariant, making influence impossible.
Correction: The objection does not deny that a cause could affect something; it argues that the three conceivable loci are invariant, thus the cause would have no effect.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that the three conceivable loci of causal influence—quiddity, existence, and qualification—are all invariant, thereby challenging the very need for a cause.

Non-existence is relational, not absolute nothingness
The author explains the relational nature of non-existence for possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that non-existence (ʿadam) of a possible being is not an absolute nothing but a relational absence relative to a cause, and why it does not require an effecter.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not nothing; it is the absence of a cause for a possible being.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in Islamic theology about the nature of non-existence. While non-existence appears to be pure negation, the author shows that it is always relative to a possible being and its cause. This distinction resolves confusion about whether non-existence itself needs a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the main claim
The author claims that non-existence does not need an effecter because it is not fit to be an effect. Find this part and underline it.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'pure negation'
The author distinguishes between pure negation as absolute nothingness and pure negation as relational absence. Grasp this distinction—it is key.
Relational non-existence is attributed to a possible being, so it is not completely unrelated to existence.
4
Connect to causality
The mind judges non-existence based on the absence of a cause. Reflect on why this makes sense: if the cause is missing, the effect remains non-existent.
This shows that non-existence is derivative and not a positive entity.
5
Apply the concept
Try to explain in your own words why non-existence does not need a cause, using the distinction from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن لكن لا يحتاج العدم إلى المؤثر لعدم صلوحه لذلك بخلاف الوجود فإن المقتضى فيه سالم عن المانع وإن صلح أثراً منعنا بطلان اللازم وهو ظاهر وتحقيقه أنه وإن كان نفياً صرفاً بمعنى أنه ليس له شائبة الوجود العيني لكن ليس نفياً صرفاً بمعنى أن لا يضاف إلى ما يتصف بالوجود بل هو عدم مضاف إلى الممكن الوجود فيستند إلى عدم علة وجوده بمعنى احتياجه إليه عند العقل حيث يحكم بأنه إنما بقى عدمه الأصلي أو اتصف بعدمه الطارىء بناء على عدم وجوده مستمراً أو طارئاً
Existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of the possible [being], but non-existence does not need an effecter because it is not fit for that, unlike existence. For the requisite in it is free from the impediment, and if it is fit as an effect we deny the necessity of the consequent, and that is apparent. Its verification is that although it is pure negation, meaning it has no tinge of external existence, yet it is not pure negation, meaning it is not attributed to what is qualified by existence. Rather, it is non-existence added to the possible existent, so it rests upon the non-existence of the cause of its existence, meaning its need for it in the mind, where the mind judges that it remained in its original non-existence or became qualified by accidental non-existence, based on the non-existence of its existence continuously or accidentally.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage defines the status of non-existence (ʿadam) for a possible being (mumkin). The author clarifies that non-existence does not require an effecter (muʾaththir) because it is not a positive reality; existence does require one. He then explains that non-existence is not pure negation (nafyan ṣirfan) because it is always relative to a possible being—it is the absence of a cause for that being's existence. The mind judges that the non-existence persists or occurs based on the absence of existence, whether continuous or accidental.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd)
عدم (ʿadam)
ممكن (mumkin)
مؤثر (muʾaththir)
نفي صرف (nafy ṣirf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is an absolute nothing, a pure non-being with no connection to anything.
Correction: Non-existence is not absolute nothing; it is relational: it is the absence of a cause for a possible being, and the mind attributes it to that being based on the lack of its cause.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does non-existence not need an effecter?
A) Because non-existence is a pure negation with no relation to existence.
B) Because non-existence is not fit to be an effect.
C) Because non-existence is a positive quality.
D) Because the possible being does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence of a possible being is not absolute nothingness but the absence of its cause, and thus does not itself require a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence right now is only sustained by God's continuous causality, what would it mean for you to live in constant awareness of that dependence? Would it change how you start your day tomorrow?
?
When you reflect on a sin you committed, do you treat it as a 'thing' with its own power, or do you see it as the absence of good and the absence of God's cause? How does that distinction affect the sincerity of your repentance?
?
Non-existence is not absolute nothingness but the absence of a cause. How does this truth confront your fear of death or your anxiety about losing something dear to you? What practical step can you take today to anchor your heart in the Causer rather than the caused?

First Objection: Non-Existence as a Cause – Resolved
Objection that non-existence cannot be cause; answer that it is not pure negation and distinctions exist in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of non-existence serving as a cause in mental judgment, rebutting the objection that non-existence cannot be a cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a cause in the mind, not in external reality.

This passage tackles a central objection in kalam about causation: how can non-existence be a cause? The objector argues that a cause must be existential, but the author explains that non-existence can function as a cause within the mind's judgments, without implying external causation. Follow the argument carefully to see how distinctions among non-existences are established in reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'فإن قيل...') to understand the objector's claim that non-existence cannot be a cause for two reasons: the cause must be existential, and non-existences are indistinguishable.
The objector assumes causation requires existence and distinctness.
2
Identify the author's main replies
Read the author's response ('قلنا...') and note the three key points: (1) the universal concept of non-existence is not purely non-existential, (2) the opposite of non-existential is not necessarily existential, and (3) non-existences are distinct in the mind.
The author does not accept the premises as stated.
3
Understand the distinction between mental and external causation
Focus on the last sentence: the author clarifies that this mental causation does not lead to 'closure of proving the Creator' because that proof concerns external reality, not mental judgments.
This distinction is crucial for the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل العدم لا يصلح علة لأن العلة وجودية لكونها نقيض اللاعلية العدمية فيفتقر إلى موصوف وجودي ولأنه لا تمايز في الإعدام فلا يصلح بعضها علة وبعضها معلولاً قلنا مجرد صورة السلب أو الصدق على المعدوم في الجملة لا تقتضي كون المفهوم الكلي عدمياً بجميع جزئياته ولو سلم فنقيض العدمي لا يلزم أن يكون وجودياً وقد سبق مثل ذلك وعدم تمايز الإعدام ممنوع التحقيق أن تساوي طرفي الممكن إلا في العقل فالمرجح لا يكون إلا وعدم العلة أو عدم الممكن ليس نفياً صرفاً بل كل منهما ثابت في العقل ممتاز عن الآخر فيصلح أحدهما علة للآخر في حكم العقل ولا يلزم عنه صلوح عليته للوجود ليلزم انسداد إثبات الصانع لأن ذلك إنما يكون بحسب الخارج
For if it is said: Non-existence is not fit to be a cause, because the cause is existential, being the opposite of non-existential causality, so it needs an existential subject, and because there is no distinction among non-existences, so some cannot be cause and some effect. We say: The mere form of negation or truth about the non-existent in general does not necessitate that the universal concept is non-existential in all its particulars. And if conceded, the opposite of non-existential is not necessarily existential. This has preceded. And the non-distinction of non-existences is denied. The verification is that the two sides of the possible are equal only in the mind, so the preponderant is only non-existence of the cause, or non-existence of the possible is not pure negation, but both are established in the mind distinct from each other, so one is fit to be a cause for the other in the judgment of the mind, and from this does not follow its fitness for causing existence to necessitate blockage of proving the Creator, because that is only with respect to the external.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against considering non-existence as a cause. The objector argues that a cause must be existential, non-existences are indistinguishable, so no causation holds. The author answers that the universal concept of non-existence is not purely non-existential in all instances; its opposite is not necessarily existential. He denies that non-existences lack distinction, explaining that the two sides of a possible are equal only in the mind, and there the preponderant (the absence of a cause) is distinct from the possible's non-existence. Thus one can be a cause for the other in mental judgment without implying a real causal relation in the external world, which would block the proof of the Creator.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla)
عدم (ʿadam)
موصوف (mawṣūf)
تمييز (tamyīz)
عقل (ʿaql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence cannot be a cause in any sense whatsoever.
Correction: The author shows that non-existence can serve as a cause in the mind's judgment, though not in external reality.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that non-existences are indistinguishable?
A) He concedes that they are indistinguishable.
B) He argues they are distinguished in the external world.
C) He denies the claim and says they are distinguished in the mind.
D) He says the distinction is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can function as a cause within mental judgments, and this does not undermine the proof of the Creator, which concerns external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author distinguishes mental causality from external causality to protect the proof of the Creator. Second Objection: Where Does the Effect Occur?
Objection and answer about effecter's role in possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possible's need for an effecter is answered by distinguishing the effect in the essence (actualization) and in existence (bestowal), without changing the quiddity.

Core Idea
Effect doesn't change essence; it actualizes it.

This passage addresses a key objection against the need for an effecter: if a possible needs an effecter, the effect must be in its essence, existence, or qualification—all seemingly impossible. The author reframes the objection by explaining that the effect is in making the essence actualized or in bestowing existence, not in changing the essence or existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage which lists the three possible loci of effect (essence, existence, qualification) and states that all are invalid based on previous arguments.
Notice the concise enumeration: 'إما في ماهية الممكن أو وجوده أو موصوفيته بالوجود'.
2
Identify the answer
Read the author's response starting with 'والجواب'. The answer clarifies that the effect is in the essence by making it actualized, not by making it an essence, or in specific existence by obtaining it for the essence.
The key phrase is 'التأثير في الماهية بأن يجعلها متحققة'.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
Understand the distinction between making something an essence (which is impossible as essence is self-identical) and making it actualized (which is the effecter's role). This resolves the objection.
This reflects the broader principle that the effecter does not change quiddities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الممكن لو احتاج إلى مؤثر فتأثيره إما في ماهية الممكن أو وجوده أو موصوفيته بالوجود إذ لا يعقل غير ذلك والكل باطل لما مر في نفي شيئية المعدوم ومجعولية الماهية من أن الماهية ماهية والوجود وجود والموصوفية موصوفية سواء وجد الغير أو لم يوجد وأن الوجود حال لا تأثير فيه وأن الموصوفية أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان والجواب أن التأثير في الماهية بأن يجعلها متحققة لا بأن يجعلها ماهية أو في الوجود الخاص بأن يحصله للماهية لا بأن يجعله وجوداً
And among them: If the possible needs an effecter, then its effect is either in the essence of the possible or its existence or its being qualified by existence, since nothing else is conceivable. And all are invalid because of what has passed in the denial of the thingness of the non-existent and the effectibility of the essence, that the essence is essence, existence is existence, and being qualified is being qualified, whether the other exists or not, and that existence is a state with no effect, and that being qualified is a relational matter with no reality in external things. The answer: The effect is in the essence by making it actualized, not by making it an essence, or in the specific existence by obtaining it for the essence, not by making it existence.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: if the possible needs an effecter, the effect can only be in the essence, existence, or being qualified by existence—all deemed invalid per previous arguments. The author answers that the effect is in the essence by making it actualized (mutḥaqqiqa), not by changing its quiddity; or in the specific existence by obtaining it for the essence, not by making it existence itself.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya)
وجود (wujūd)
موصوفية (mawṣūfiyya)
تأثير (taʾthīr)
مؤثر (muʾaththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Effecting changes the quiddity of the possible.
Correction: Effecting makes the essence actualized, not changes its nature.

Checkpoint
According to the author, where does the effecter's effect occur?
A) In the essence by making it a different essence.
B) In the existence by creating existence itself.
C) In the essence by making it actualized.
D) In the qualification by making it a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
The effecter's role is to actualize the essence and bestow existence, not to alter the essence's quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You are a possible that only exists through the effecter's actualization. Does the way you live your daily life reflect that you are entirely contingent upon God, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would you need to stop doing to truly embody this dependency?
?
The effecter does not change your nature but bestows specific existence upon you. What specific existence has God given you that you are not living up to, and what step will you take today to actualize that potential for His sake?

The Third Objection: Infinite Regress of Effecting and Need Answered
Objection that effecting and need lead to regress; answer that they are relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress regarding effecting and need is resolved by recognizing these concepts as relational rather than real attributes.

Core Idea
Effecting and need are relational concepts, not real entities, so they do not cause regress.

This passage addresses a classical objection against causal chains: if an effecter's 'effecting' and a possible being's 'need' were real, they would themselves require effecters, leading to an infinite regress. The answer is that these are relational (iʿtibārī) concepts, not independently existing attributes. Understanding this protects the coherence of causal reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the claim that effecting and need would lead to infinite regress if they were real.
Notice that the objection also dismisses an attempted rebuttal that they are identical.
2
Understand the answer
The author answers that effecting and need are relational (iʿtibārī), not real attributes.
This means they are mental considerations, not extra entities.
3
Analyze why regress is avoided
Relational concepts do not require their own effecters; they depend on the relata.
The effecter remains an effecter and the needy remains needy relationally.
4
Generalize the principle
Many theological terms (e.g., creation, lordship) are relational; misinterpreting them as real leads to regress.
This is a key methodological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه لو وجدت مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن أو احتياج الممكن إليه لكان كل منهما أمراً ممكناً له مؤثر واحتياج ويتسلسل ولا يندفع بأن مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن واحتياج الاحتياج عينه لأن ذلك يمتنع في الأمور التي بها تحقق في الأعيان الجواب أن يكون المؤثرية أو الاحتياج اعتبارياً لا ينافي كون المؤثر مؤثراً أو المحتاج محتاجاً على ما سبق غير مرة
And among them: If the effecting of the effecter in the possible or the need of the possible for it existed, then each would be a possible thing having an effecter and a need, leading to infinite regress. And it is not repelled by saying that the effecting of the effecter in the possible and the need of the need are identical, because that is impossible for things that have reality in external entities. The answer: That the effecting or the need is relational, not contradicting the effecter being an effecter or the needy being needy, as has preceded multiple times.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage raises an objection about infinite regress: if the effecting (muʾthiriyya) and the need (iḥtiyāj) were real, they would themselves be possible and require further effecters, leading to regress. The answer is that effecting and need are relational (iʿtibārī) concepts, not real entities, so they do not cause regress. The effecter remains an effecter and the needy remains needy in a relational sense.

Key Terms
مؤثرية (muʾthiriyya)
احتياج (iḥtiyāj)
اعتباري (iʿtibārī)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that effecting and need are real attributes that exist independently, leading to the objection of infinite regress.
Correction: The author clarifies that these are relational (iʿtibārī) concepts, not real entities, so they do not cause regress.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid infinite regress in the effecting and need?
A) By saying the effecter and need are identical.
B) By denying that the possible needs an effecter.
C) By stating that effecting and need are relational, not real.
D) By positing a first cause.

Key Takeaway
Effecting and need are relational concepts, not real attributes, thus avoiding infinite regress and preserving causal coherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat your own 'effecting'—your actions and choices—as if they were independent realities deserving of their own causes, or do you see them as relational, dependent on Allah? How would your behavior change if you truly felt that your actions are not self-standing but merely relational to His will?
?
Consider the things you feel you need: health, wealth, relationships. Are you clinging to these needs as if they are real attributes of yourself that require their own satisfiers, or are you aware that your need itself is just a relational state pointing to the only true Satisfier? What would it take to make that awareness a lived reality?
?
When you reflect on the concept of regress—that if every effect itself were a cause, there would be no end—do you feel the weight of that intellectual problem? Or have you ever felt trapped in an endless cycle of seeking causes for your own needs, never resting in the One who needs no cause? What step can you take today to break that cycle and rest in the relational truth that your need is not an independent entity but a pointer to Allah?

Existence as a State and Being-Described as Conceptual
First objection and answer about existence and being-described.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence has no effect and being-described is conceptual, and the author's answer that effect is in the quiddity by actualization, not by changing its essence.

Core Idea
Effect occurs in the quiddity by making it actual, not by altering what it is.

The first objection challenges the idea that existence itself is effective and that being-described is a real attribute. In response, the author clarifies the precise locus of causation: effect pertains to the quiddity's actualization, not to its definition. This distinction is crucial for understanding causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part: existence is a state without effect; being-described is conceptual with no external reality.
Notice the two claims are separate but related.
2
Read the answer
The answer states that effect is in the quiddity by making it actual, not by making it a quiddity, and similarly for existence.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Understand that causation does not change the essence of things but brings them into actuality.
This connects to broader themes of causal efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن الوجود حال (۱) لا تأثير فيه، وأن الموصوفية أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان . والجواب أن التأثير في الماهية بأن يجعلها متحققة. لا بأن يجعلها ماهية أو في الوجود الخاص بأن يحصله (۲) للماهية. لا بأن يجعله وجوداً، ومنها أنه لو وجدت مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن، أو احتياج (۳) الممكن إليه، لكان كل منهما أمراً ممكناً له مؤثر واحتياج (4) ، ويتسلسل، ولا يندفع بأن مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن واحتياج الاحتياج عينه لأن ذلك ) يمتنع في الأمور التي بها تحقق في الأعيان . الجواب أن يكون المؤثرية أو الاحتياج اعتبارياً، لا ينافي كون المؤثر مؤثراً، أو المحتاج محتاجاً على ما سبق غير مرة. وما يقال: من أنه لو حصل في العقل دون الخارج كان جهلاً لانتفاء المطابقة، وأن كلا منهما صفة حاصلة قبل الأذهان، فيستحيل قيامها بالذهن . فجوابه . أن عدم المطابقة للخارج إنما يكون جهلاً، إذا حكم العقل بالثبوت في الخارج، ولم يثبت، وأن الحاصل قبل الأذهان هو كون الشيء بحيث إذا تعقله الذهن حصل فيه معقول هو المؤثرية أو الحاجة .
And that existence is a state (1) having no effect, and that being-described is a conceptual matter having no realization in external entities. The answer: that the effect is in the quiddity by making it actual, not by making it a quiddity, or in the particular existence by bringing it about for the quiddity, not by making it existence. And among them is that if the effectuality of the effectual cause in the possible, or the need of the possible for it, existed, then each of them would be a possible thing having an effectual cause and a need, leading to infinite regress; and it is not repelled by saying that the effectuality of the effectual cause in the possible and the need of the need are the same thing, because that is impossible in things by which there is realization in external entities. The answer: that effectuality or need is conceptual, not contradicting that the effectual cause is effectual or the needy is needy, as has preceded many times. And what is said: that if it occurs in the mind without the external, it would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence, and that each of them is an attribute occurring before minds, so it is impossible for them to subsist in the mind. Its answer: that lack of correspondence to the external is only ignorance when the mind judges that it exists externally and it does not; and that what occurs before minds is that the thing is such that when the mind conceives it, a concept occurs in it which is effectuality or need.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers regarding the effectiveness of the effectual cause and the need of the possible. The first objection states that existence is a mere state without effect, and being-described is a conceptual matter with no external realization. The author answers that the effect is in the quiddity by making it actual, not by changing its quiddity, and similarly in bringing about particular existence, not by making existence per se. The second objection concerns infinite regress: if effectuality and need were real, they would themselves be possible things requiring another effectual cause and need, leading to infinite regress. The answer is that effectuality and need are conceptual (i‘tibārī), not real attributes, so they do not necessitate infinite regress. A further objection claims that if these concepts exist only in the mind, judgments about them would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence with external reality. The answer distinguishes: ignorance only occurs if the mind judges something to exist externally when it does not; but here the concepts in the mind correspond to the actual state of affairs that the effectual cause is such that when conceived, the concept of effectuality arises, and similarly for need.

Key Terms
وجود
حال
موصوفية
تأثير
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if existence has no effect, then the cause is not needed.
Correction: The cause affects the quiddity by actualizing it; existence itself is not the locus of effect but the result.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the effect of the cause actually do?
A) It changes the quiddity of a thing.
B) It makes the quiddity actual.
C) It creates existence from nothing.
D) It eliminates the need for a cause.

Key Takeaway
Effect in quiddity is actualization, not essential change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your character traits are like a quiddity that can only be actualized, not changed, then what does that mean for the habits you are trying to build or break? Does this make you feel helpless or empowered? How will you ACT this week to actualize the good that is already latent in you?
?

?
You believe that your deeds have real effects. But do you live as if your intentions are mere conceptual states? If your being-described (موصوفية) as a believer is only conceptual without actualization, what will you change TODAY to make your faith real in the world?

Infinite Regress of Effectuality and Need
Second objection and answer on infinite regress of effectuality and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection of infinite regress against effectuality and need fails when they are considered conceptual rather than real attributes.

Core Idea
Effectuality and need are conceptual, so they do not generate an infinite chain.

The second objection argues that if effectuality and need were real, they would themselves require causes, leading to infinite regress. The author answers by explaining that these are conceptual (i‘tibārī) attributes, not real entities, thus no regress follows. This distinction is a key tool in Islamic theology to avoid infinite regress arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
If effectuality (mu'aththiriyya) of the cause and need (iḥtiyāj) of the possible were real, they would be possible things requiring their own cause and need, leading to infinite regress.
2
Read the counter-argument
The objector says it's not repelled by claiming they are identical because that's impossible for real things.
3
Read the author's answer
The author asserts that effectuality and need are conceptual (i‘tibārī), not real, so no infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the significance
Conceptual attributes do not require extra-mental causes, so they can be predicated without regress.
This is a standard position in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن الوجود حال (۱) لا تأثير فيه، وأن الموصوفية أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان . والجواب أن التأثير في الماهية بأن يجعلها متحققة. لا بأن يجعلها ماهية أو في الوجود الخاص بأن يحصله (۲) للماهية. لا بأن يجعله وجوداً، ومنها أنه لو وجدت مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن، أو احتياج (۳) الممكن إليه، لكان كل منهما أمراً ممكناً له مؤثر واحتياج (4) ، ويتسلسل، ولا يندفع بأن مؤثرية المؤثر في الممكن واحتياج الاحتياج عينه لأن ذلك ) يمتنع في الأمور التي بها تحقق في الأعيان . الجواب أن يكون المؤثرية أو الاحتياج اعتبارياً، لا ينافي كون المؤثر مؤثراً، أو المحتاج محتاجاً على ما سبق غير مرة. وما يقال: من أنه لو حصل في العقل دون الخارج كان جهلاً لانتفاء المطابقة، وأن كلا منهما صفة حاصلة قبل الأذهان، فيستحيل قيامها بالذهن . فجوابه . أن عدم المطابقة للخارج إنما يكون جهلاً، إذا حكم العقل بالثبوت في الخارج، ولم يثبت، وأن الحاصل قبل الأذهان هو كون الشيء بحيث إذا تعقله الذهن حصل فيه معقول هو المؤثرية أو الحاجة .
And that existence is a state (1) having no effect, and that being-described is a conceptual matter having no realization in external entities. The answer: that the effect is in the quiddity by making it actual, not by making it a quiddity, or in the particular existence by bringing it about for the quiddity, not by making it existence. And among them is that if the effectuality of the effectual cause in the possible, or the need of the possible for it, existed, then each of them would be a possible thing having an effectual cause and a need, leading to infinite regress; and it is not repelled by saying that the effectuality of the effectual cause in the possible and the need of the need are the same thing, because that is impossible in things by which there is realization in external entities. The answer: that effectuality or need is conceptual, not contradicting that the effectual cause is effectual or the needy is needy, as has preceded many times. And what is said: that if it occurs in the mind without the external, it would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence, and that each of them is an attribute occurring before minds, so it is impossible for them to subsist in the mind. Its answer: that lack of correspondence to the external is only ignorance when the mind judges that it exists externally and it does not; and that what occurs before minds is that the thing is such that when the mind conceives it, a concept occurs in it which is effectuality or need.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers regarding the effectiveness of the effectual cause and the need of the possible. The first objection states that existence is a mere state without effect, and being-described is a conceptual matter with no external realization. The author answers that the effect is in the quiddity by making it actual, not by changing its quiddity, and similarly in bringing about particular existence, not by making existence per se. The second objection concerns infinite regress: if effectuality and need were real, they would themselves be possible things requiring another effectual cause and need, leading to infinite regress. The answer is that effectuality and need are conceptual (i‘tibārī), not real attributes, so they do not necessitate infinite regress. A further objection claims that if these concepts exist only in the mind, judgments about them would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence with external reality. The answer distinguishes: ignorance only occurs if the mind judges something to exist externally when it does not; but here the concepts in the mind correspond to the actual state of affairs that the effectual cause is such that when conceived, the concept of effectuality arises, and similarly for need.

Key Terms
مؤثرية
احتياج
ممكن
تسلسل
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that conceptual means imaginary or false.
Correction: Conceptual (i‘tibārī) means real in the mind but not extra-mental, yet it accurately describes the state of affairs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that effectuality and need do not lead to infinite regress?
A) Because they are identical to the cause and needy.
B) Because they are conceptual, not real extra-mental entities.
C) Because infinite regress only applies to eternal things.
D) Because the cause itself prevents regress.

Key Takeaway
Effectuality and need are conceptual, avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own attributes—like your role as a parent, student, or friend—do you sometimes treat them as real, independent entities that need to be justified? How does understanding them as conceptual (while still real in effect) free you from unnecessary anxiety or pride?
?
The objector assumed that if effectuality were real it would lead to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, are there chains of dependency you feel trapped in? How does recognizing that conceptual attributes do not cause regress help you break that mental chain and rest in Allah's sufficiency?
?
This lesson teaches that a cause can be truly effectual even if the attribute 'effectuality' is conceptual. Where in your relationship with Allah have you confused the conceptual with the real? What would change if you saw your own dependence on Allah as real but your conceptual narratives about it as secondary?

Conceptual Attributes and Knowledge Correspondence
Third objection and answer on conceptual attributes and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that conceptual attributes lead to ignorance and the author's distinction between correct and incorrect judgments of correspondence.

Core Idea
Ignorance arises only when the mind judges existence externally where there is none, not from conceptual attributes.

The third objection claims that if effectuality and need are only in the mind, then judgments about them would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence. The author answers by clarifying that ignorance occurs only when the mind makes a false judgment about external existence, not when it correctly conceives a conceptual attribute that has a basis in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
If effectuality/need occur only in the mind, not externally, then judgments about them would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence. Also, they are attributes that exist before minds, so they cannot subsist in the mind.
2
Read the first part of the answer
Lack of correspondence only yields ignorance if the mind judges something to exist externally when it does not.
3
Read the second part
What exists before minds is that the thing is such that when conceived, the concept of effectuality or need arises in the mind.
4
Synthesize
Thus, conceptual attributes correspond to real dispositions in the object, so they are not ignorant.

The Cause of the Possible's Need: Contingency or Origination?
The sixth investigation debates whether contingency or origination causes the possible's need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether contingency (imkān) or origination (ḥudūth) is the cause of the possible's need for a cause, and the author's clarification that contingency is a rational observation, not a real attribute.

Core Idea
The possible's need for a cause is judged by reason observing its contingency, not by a real attribute.

This lesson examines the sixth investigation of the text, which tackles a fundamental question in Islamic theology: what exactly necessitates a contingent being's need for a cause? The author presents his view that it is contingency itself, then reports the opposing view of many mutakallimun who argue for origination. A key clarification is made: contingency is not a real property but a rational observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two positions presented and the partial answer.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full answer continues on the next page.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the author's claim that contingency is the necessitator, and the opposing claim from the mutakallimun that origination is the necessitator. Note the supporting reasoning for each.
The author also discusses whether origination could be independent, a condition, or a part.
3
Understand the author's answer
The author clarifies that he does not mean contingency is a real attribute that produces need; rather, reason observes contingency and judges the need. This is a key distinction.
The answer is incomplete due to the page break, but the main point is clear.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that contingency is a real cause. The author explicitly corrects this by stating it is a rational observation.
Use the checkpoint to test your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس في موجب احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر (قال : المبحث السادس : العقل بحكم الاحتياج (۱) بمجرد ملاحظة كون الذات (۲) غير مقتضية للوجود والعدم (۳)، فيكون المحوج هو الإمكان . الحدوث مستقلاً (٤) أو شرطاً أو شطراً (*) كيف . والحدوث صفة للوجود المتأخر عن التأثر المتأخر عن الاحتياج؟ وكثير من المتكلمين عكسوا الدعوى، والدليل (٦) والابطال. فقالوا : العقل يحكم بالاحتياج بمجرد ملاحظة أن الشيء لم يكن فكان، فيكون المحوج هو الحدوث لا الإمكان (۷) ، كيف (۸) وهو كيفية نسبة الماهية إلى الوجود المتأخر عن الاحتياج. الجواب : بأنا لا نعني أن الإمكان يتحقق فيوجب احتياجاً، بل إن العقل يلاحظ الإمكان فيحكم بالاحتياج (۹) ، كما يقال علة الاحتياج إلى الخير هـو
The sixth investigation on the cause of the possible's need for an effectual cause. He said: The sixth investigation: reason judges need (1) simply by observing that the essence (2) does not necessitate existence and non-existence (3), so the necessitator is contingency. Origination independently (4) or as a condition or a part (*) how. And origination is an attribute of existence posterior to being affected posterior to need? And many of the mutakallimun reversed the claim, and the proof (6) and the refutation. They said: reason judges need simply by observing that the thing was not and then it was, so the necessitator is origination, not contingency (7), how (8) while it is a mode of the relation of the quiddity to existence posterior to need. The answer: that we do not mean that contingency is realized and then necessitates a need, but rather that reason observes contingency and then judges the need (9), as it is said the cause of the need for good is...
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth investigation (al-mabḥath al-sādis) on what necessitates the possible's need for an effectual cause. The author (presumably al-Taftazani) states the position of those who hold that the cause of need is contingency (imkān): reason judges that a possible thing needs a cause simply by observing that its essence is neutral between existence and non-existence. They also consider whether origination (ḥudūth) could be the cause independently or as a condition or part, but note that origination is an attribute of existence that comes after being affected, which itself is after need, so it cannot be the cause. Then the author reports that many mutakallimun reversed the claim and presented a proof and refutation: they argue that reason judges need by observing that the thing did not exist and then existed, so the necessitator is origination, not contingency. They question how contingency could be the cause, since it is the mode of the quiddity's relation to existence that is posterior to need. The answer is a clarification: the author does not mean that contingency is realized as an attribute that then produces need; rather, reason observes the state of contingency (i.e., the quiddity's essential non-necessitation) and thereby judges that the thing needs a cause. This judgment is not based on a real attribute of contingency but on a rational inference. The analogy is given: just as one says the cause of the need for good is [something—the text cuts off].

محوج
عقل
ماهية
وجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency is a real attribute that produces the need for a cause, like an effective cause.
Correction: The author clarifies that contingency is not a real attribute that causes need; rather, reason observes the state of contingency and then judges that the possible needs a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, how does contingency cause the need for an effectual cause?
A) Contingency is a real attribute that makes the possible need a cause.
B) Reason observes contingency and then judges that the possible needs a cause.
C) Contingence produces need directly as an efficient cause.
D) Contingency is identical to the need itself.

Key Takeaway
The need of the possible for a cause is judged by reason upon observing its contingency, not because contingency is a real attribute that produces need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live each moment aware that your very existence depends on Allah's will, or do you unconsciously attribute your continued existence to your own efforts and natural processes? How would your daily actions change if you genuinely believed that contingency alone makes you utterly dependent on Allah at every instant?
?
When you reflect on your own origin—that you were not and then you were—is your immediate reaction to credit secondary causes (parents, biology) or to see the direct hand of Allah? What does this reveal about where your heart's reliance truly lies?
?
The mutakallimun argued that origination is the cause of need because it is observable. But the author insists that the rational ground is deeper: contingency itself. Examine your own reasoning: do you only feel your need for Allah when faced with a clear beginning (e.g., birth) or do you sense your constant need even in your ongoing existence? Commit to a specific moment today to pause and remind yourself of your contingency.

The Cause of Need: Imkan vs Huduth
Al-Taftazani presents the debate on the cause of need and reconciles it as an intellectual judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on why a possible thing needs a cause, and the reconciliation that it is an intellectual judgment.

Core Idea
The need for a cause is a judgment of the intellect, not an external property.

Al-Taftazani begins by reviewing the established fact that the possible needs a cause, but then explains the disagreement over the reason. The philosophers attribute it to possibility (imkan), the early theologians to origination (huduth), and some to both. He presents their arguments and finally clarifies the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the positions
Notice the four views mentioned: (1) philosophers: imkan, (2) early theologians: huduth, (3) some: imkan together with huduth, (4) some: conditioned by huduth. Write them down.
This sets up the debate.
3
Understand the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue that the intellect judges need when it sees a thing is not necessary in itself, regardless of whether it is preceded by nonexistence. They also argue against huduth as causing need because huduth is posterior to existence, leading to circularity.
Note the use of 'intellectual observation'.
4
Understand the theologians' counter-argument
The theologians argue that need is caused by huduth: when the intellect sees a thing comes after nonexistence, it judges need. They reject imkan because it is a relation posterior to existence.
Contrast with the philosophers.
5
Grasp the reconciliation
Al-Taftazani states the truth (al-haqq): the cause of need is not in external reality but is an intellectual judgment based on observing imkan or huduth. The debate over invalidation is sophistry; the later theologians' view on affirmation is more evident.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق أن الممكن محتاج إلى السبب. إلا أن ذلك عند الفلاسفة وبعض المتكلمين لإمكانه، وعند قدماء المتكلمين لحدوثه، وقيل لإمكانه مع الحدوث، وقيل بشرط الحدوث. احتجت الفلاسفة على دعواهم بأن العقل إذا لاحظ كون الشيء غير مقتضي الوجود أو العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته حكم بأن وجوده أو عدمه لا يكون إلا بسبب خارج وهو معنى الاحتياج، سواء لاحظ كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم أو لم يلاحظ. واحتجوا على إبطال مذهب المخالف بأن الحدوث وصف للوجود ومتأخر عنه لكونه عبارة عن مسبوقية الوجود بالعدم، والوجود متأخر عن تأثير المؤثر، وهو عن الاحتياج إليه، وهو عن علة الاحتياج وجزئها وشرطها، فلو كان الحدوث علة للاحتياج أو جزأها أو شرطها، لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب. وعارضهم بعض المتكلمين، فقالوا: سبب الاحتياج هو الحدوث، لأن العقل إذا لاحظ كون الشيء مما يوجد بعد العدم، حكم باحتياجه إلى علة تخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود، وإن لم يلاحظ كونه غير ضروري الوجود والعدم. ولا يجوز أن يكون هو الإمكان، لأنه كيفية لنسبة الوجود إلى الماهية فيتأخر عن الوجود المتأخر عن التأثير المتأخر عن الاحتياج إلى المؤثر. والحق أن هذه العلة إنما هي بحسب العقل بمعنى أنه يلاحظ الإمكان أو الحدوث فيحكم بالاحتياج، كما يقال علة المحصول في الخير هو التحيز لا بحسب الخارج بأن يتحقق الإمكان أو الحدوث فيوجد الاحتياج. وبهذا يظهر أن كلام الفريقين في الإبطال مغالطة. وأما في الإثبات، فكلام المتأخرين أظهر، وبالقبول أجدر.

واعترض بأنه لو كان علة الاحتياج إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان أو الحدوث، وهما لازمان للممكن والحادث لزم احتياجهما حالة البقاء لدوام المعلول بدوام العلة، واللازم باطل لأن التأثير حينئذ إما في الوجود وقد حصل بمجرد وجود المؤثر فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل بحصول سابق، وإما في البقاء أو أمر آخر متجدد، وهو تأثير في غير الباقي، أعني الممكن والحادث فيلزم استغناؤهما عن المؤثر. وفي كون الإمكان علة الاحتياج فساد آخر، وهو احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر حال عدمه السابق، مع أنه نفي محض أزلي لا يعقل له مؤثر. الجواب أن معنى احتياج الممكن أو الحادث إلى المؤثر، توقف حصول الوجود له أو العدم أو استمرارهما على تحقق أمر أو انتفائه، بمعنى امتناعه بدون ذلك، وهو معنى دوام الأثر بدوام المؤثر. وإذا تحققت فاستمرار الوجود، أعني البقاء، ليس إلا وجوداً مأخوذاً بالإضافة إلى الزمان الثاني، وصحة قولنا وجد فلم يبق ولم يستمر لا يدل إلا على مغايرة البقاء لمطلق الوجود ولا نزاع في ذلك.
It has already preceded that the possible [thing] is in need of the cause. Except that this, according to the philosophers and some theologians, is due to its possibility, and according to the early theologians, due to its origination, and it was said [it is] due to its possibility along with origination, and it was said conditioned by origination. The philosophers argued for their claim that the intellect, when it observes that a thing does not necessitate existence or nonexistence with respect to its essence, judges that its existence or nonexistence only occurs through an external cause, and that is the meaning of need, whether it observes that it is preceded by nonexistence or does not observe. And they argued for the invalidation of the opponent's view that origination is a description of existence and posterior to it, because it is an expression of the precedence of existence by nonexistence, and existence is posterior to the effect of the agent, and it [the effect] is posterior to the need for it [the agent], and it [the need] is posterior to the cause of need, its part, and its condition. So if origination were the cause of need, or its part, or its condition, it would entail the thing being posterior to itself by degrees. And some theologians opposed them, and they said: the cause of need is origination, because the intellect, when it observes that a thing is of the kind that comes into existence after nonexistence, judges its need for a cause that brings it out from nonexistence into existence, even if it does not observe that it is not necessary in existence and nonexistence. And it is not permissible that it be possibility, because it is a modality of the relation of existence to essence, and thus it is posterior to existence, which is posterior to the effect, which is posterior to the need for the agent. And the truth is that this cause is only according to the intellect, in the sense that it observes possibility or origination and judges the need, just as it is said: the cause of the product in kheir is determination [in space], not according to external [reality] such that possibility or origination are realized and then need comes to exist. And by this it becomes apparent that the speech of the two groups concerning invalidation is sophistry. As for affirmation, the speech of the later [theologians] is more evident and more worthy of acceptance.

And it was objected that if the cause of need for the agent were possibility or origination, and they are inseparable from the possible and the originated, it would entail their need in the state of persistence, because of the permanence of the effect due to the permanence of the cause. And the consequent is false because the effect then is either upon existence, and it has already occurred by the mere existence of the agent, so it entails obtaining the obtained by a prior attainment; or it is upon persistence or some other renewed thing, and that is an effect upon what is not subsisting, namely the possible and the originated, so it would entail their independence from the agent. And concerning possibility being the cause of need there is another corruption, which is the need of the possible for the agent in its prior state of nonexistence, while it is a pure eternal negation for which no agent is conceivable. The answer: the meaning of the need of the possible or the originated for the agent is the dependence of the attainment of existence, or nonexistence, or their continuation for it upon the realization of a matter or its negation, in the sense of its impossibility without that. And that is the meaning of the permanence of the effect due to the permanence of the agent. And if you realize that, then the continuation of existence, i.e., persistence, is nothing but existence taken in relation to the second moment of time, and the correctness of our saying 'it existed, then it did not persist and did not continue' only indicates the difference of persistence from absolute existence, and there is no dispute about that.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion about the cause of the need for a cause (al-ihiyaj ila al-mu'aththir). It begins by stating that it is already established that the possible (mumkin) needs a cause, but there is a difference of opinion: the philosophers (falasifa) and some mutakallimun say the need is due to its possibility (imkan), while the early mutakallimun say it is due to its origination (huduth). Others combine both. Then the philosophers present their proof: if the intellect considers a thing as not entailing existence or nonexistence by its essence, it judges that its existence or nonexistence must come from an external cause, regardless of whether it is preceded by nonexistence. They also argue against huduth as the cause: huduth is a description of existence and therefore posterior to it; if huduth were the cause of need, it would lead to infinite regress. The mutakallimun counter that the cause is huduth because the intellect, when considering a thing that comes into existence after nonexistence, judges it needs a cause to bring it out of nonexistence, even without considering its possibility. They reject imkan because it is a relation of existence to essence, which is posterior to existence, etc. The truth (al-haqq) is that both are intellectual considerations: the intellect observes either imkan or huduth and judges need accordingly, just as it is said that the cause of the product in kheir is determination in space (tahayyuz) — not in external reality. Thus the debate in negation is sophistry, but in affirmation the later scholars' (muta'akhkhirun) view is more apparent and more acceptable.

Then an objection (i'tirad) is raised: if the cause of need is imkan or huduth, which are inseparable from the possible/originated, then they would need a cause in the state of persistence (baqa'), because the effect persists with the persistence of the cause. But that is false because if the cause were to act upon existence, that would be obtaining the obtained (tahsil al-hasil), and if it acts upon persistence, that would be affecting something else, implying independence from the cause. Also, imkan as cause leads to the need of a possible thing for a cause in its prior nonexistence, which is a pure eternal negation. The answer (jawab): the meaning of need is that the attainment of existence, nonexistence, or their continuation depends on something in the sense that it is impossible without it. Continuation of existence (baqa') is just existence considered with respect to a second moment of time, and the fact that we say 'it existed, then it did not persist' only proves that baqa' is different from absolute existence, which is not disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a cause is an intrinsic property of the possible thing in external reality.
Correction: The need is a judgment of the intellect based on observing imkan or huduth; it is not an externally existing attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation presented, what is the correct view on the cause of the need for a cause?
A) It is due to possibility (imkan) alone.
B) It is due to origination (huduth) alone.
C) It is a judgment of the intellect based on observing either possibility or origination.
D) It is due to the intellect's observation of determination in space (tahayyuz).

Key Takeaway
The cause of need for a cause is not an external property but an intellectual judgment triggered by observing imkan or huduth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence, do you merely accept that you were born, or do you intellectually grasp that you, as a contingent being, could not have existed without a cause? Does that realization fill you with awe and dependence on Allah right now? If not, what is blocking it?
?
The philosophers argued that need is based on imkan, regardless of creation in time. Do you feel your need for Allah only when you think about your beginning, or do you sense it at every moment because you are inherently possible? How can you train yourself to live in that constant awareness?
?
Al-Taftazani says the cause of need is an intellectual observation, not an external property. This means your faith in God is not merely inherited but grounded in a clear rational judgment. Have you ever taken the time to personally make that judgment? If not, what is stopping you from sitting with your intellect and realizing your absolute need for Him?
